1451648537|4.0|It's odd to say that a 600 page biography isn't definitive. The writer had exclusive access to Jobs; his friends, family and employees were encouraged to participate. The author is selling the book on the basis that it's unvarnished and nuanced. This is supposed to be the definitive guide to the personality behind the inventions, brands, inspirational products, tirades and impassioned speeches.   It's a brilliant book in many respects. It's well written as would be absolutely expected from a writer of Walter Isaacson's experience and talent. It also gives us a glimpse of the generous and caring side of a man often described as a tyrant. Letting Steve speak to the reader at the end of the final chapter is inspired and Steve's words should be studied and dissected by young entrepreneurs for years to come. Don't sweat the profits, focus on making great products!  But it's strangely flawed. Other reviewers have pointed out that there are moments of Job's life that are glossed over and, perhaps, covered better elsewhere. The \\"Second Coming of Steve Jobs\\" arguably does a better job of describing his mental state when he was heading towards bankruptcy as Pixar and NeXT gobbled up his remaining resources. If you were to judge Jobs solely based on this book, the only stressful point in his life was his resignation from Apple and success was inevitable in one form or another. Working at both Apple and Pixar concurrently was tough but that's written off in about a paragraph. It's just a footnote to greater success. Is this really the case? There's little sense of the road less traveled and the soul searching he must have done. What motivated his decisions, what was his thought process like? Despite the many quotes from the author's long discussions with Steve, these mostly have the polished veneer of an Apple keynote. Isaacson was taking minutes at Steve's ultimate product launch, himself.   Perhaps we are all simply too familiar with this flawed genius and it's asking the impossible for a writer to discover something new, but that's how this book is billed. The fundamental problems in this book are   1) it lacks reflection as the author was under huge pressure to publish. Isaacson should have waited until Steve was dead, the dust had settled and gotten more objective and reflective contributions from the many interviewees in this book. Very often, we don't understand a person's character and contribution to our lives until they're gone and our minds adjust to never seeing or working with them again.      2) The author fell prey to the reality distortion field. Spinning his life as a series of stories is Steve's intellectual backyard. He owns it and it plays to his brilliant showmanship and the ability to entrance that has been the hallmark of his career.      3) Steve was dying and Isaacon simply didn't push him hard enough to get to the juice of his personality flaws. Many contradictions in his lifestyle and beliefs are laid out but we never get the psychiatrists chair treatment that his fans would like. Iaaacson couldn't arguably as, by the time he started the book, Steve was frequently ill and his longevity was uncertain. Isaacson simply couldn't risk p!ssing him off, nor did he want to hurt him. This is understandable and it's just unfortunate he didn't start the book years earlier.    4) Isaacson may not have studied the unofficial biographies or dismissed them as he wanted a fresh perspective. Fresh can be a synonym for naieve. Therefore we see a rediscovery of old narratives without the benefit of critical analysis. The author's biography of Einstein is, by contrast, revelatory and confrontational in its depiction of the 20th century's most brilliant mind.   This biography is essential reading (no mistake about it) but this is ultimately hagiography of a 20th and 21st Century iCon. What made him tick? Who knows but I suspect that if Isaacson was to rewrite the book next year he'd provide us with better insights. He has been analysed better in fiction (see his sister's \\"A Regular Guy\\") What a pity.

B005ORVN1Y|5.0|The Sector Box sets are the latest and supposedly greatest reissue for what must be one of Mercury Records' most profitable back catalogs - that of Rush, the Canadian trio many of us consider to be one of rock's greatest bands ever.  Featuring the last Terry Brown produced album (Signals) in a 5.1 channel mix, Sector 3 provides us with several of the band's greatest achievements, notably Signals itself and 1984's dark and spare Grace Under Pressure.  This box also features the fresh, appealing, melodically inventive, though occasionally gimmicky Power Windows and Hold Your Fire, which represents the zenith of the band's increasing use of keyboard and synthesizers to provide color and effect.    Most hard-core Rush fans find a considerable amount to disagree over when it comes to 80's Rush - many believe it to be too gimmicky, too keyboard intensive, they lament that Alex was essentially pieced out of many songs and, in some cases, whole albums (though that is really a stretch, especially when applied to Signals), and that the band fell victim to a penchant for keyboard technology and the fashion of the times (this was the 80's, after all...)  I grew up during this time (junior high-senior high) and bought all of these albums on vinyl upon their release, so I can't really hate them.  After 25 years, though, I can start to be more objective about them.  Some are great, even more so than when they were released - such as Signals and GUP.  Power Windows remains a strong album lyrically, but some of its effects and \\"moments\\" are growing less appealing with the passage of time.  Hold Your Fire, which I used to refer to as \\"Power Windows II\\" or \\"Son of Power Windows\\", is another very dualistic album for me, with great tracks such as Second Nature, Prime Mover, Lock and Key, and High Water being offset by Tai Shan, Time Stand Still (yes, I said it), and Turn the Page.  Finally, we have the last live Rush album of the 80's, A Show of Hands, which I now love instead of merelyliking alot.    These CD's have been completely remastered after being transferred from either the original master tapes or 192 khz copies apparently made 7 or so years ago for the band's archive.  There is a great internet article about this remastering process where the mastering engineer Andy VanDette talks about his love for Rush, the honor and privilege it was to remaster all of their albums, and the process of doing so.  For example, he notes that he tried for 3 days to use the Grace Under Pressure master tape for this set, but just could not get it to sound better than the 192 khz copy; the original tape had just deteriorated too much since 1984.  He does not mention every album specifically.  What is clear, however, is that VanDette has boosted the bass considerably on these remasters, something he alludes to in the article.  Looking at the wave forms of the songs, it's likely that some compression has been used in some places, along with the bass equalization.  Overally, there is a heavier feel to the music generally.  On Signals and the studio albums I thought this was to the good for most songs, especially for those listeners without speakers like we had in the 80's (i.e. we had very large ones).  On A Show of Hands, the new transfer is clearly better than any previous issue - this album finally sounds and feels like a live album!  It has weight, presence, and sounds much more like the Rush I remember from concerts during that time.  So is this box worth the price?  If you love Rush and you like 5.1, you might consider this a mandatory purchase.  However, many folks out in Rush land suspect that all Rush albums will be remixed in 5.1 with the different vocal takes and the other small changes you hear here.  If you only listen to CD's and or rip CD's for iPod, these are the best sounding remasters for those purposes.  It is unlikely that there will be another remastering done to the Rush catalog again, so this will likely be the final statement.  I thinkit has been well-done, and in good presentation.

B0053YGYO4|4.0|I recall seeing the video for \\"Yellow\\" back in early 2001, and turning to my wife and saying, \\"They remind me of Travis and U2.\\" Liking both bands, I went and got \\"Parachutes\\" which was not as good as I was expecting. So, I skipped \\"A Rush Of Blood To The Head\\" in 2002, until I heard \\"Clocks\\" and \\"The Scientist\\". I bought that album and felt it was an improvement, and I was becoming a Coldplay fan. I bought \\"X & Y\\" in 2005 and thought it was the band's best work until they surpassed it with 2008's \\"Viva La Vida Or Death And All His Friends\\". Now comes \\"Mylo Xyloto\\".<br /><br />Unlike other critics of Coldplay, I have felt that the band has always had there own sound, but they continually borrow from the U2 play book. I have never felt that the band was trying to be U2. Coldplay is like a U2 offspring--there are similarities, but ultimately, Chris Martin and company are working their own row. The comparisons to Travis and other bands has been set aside at this point in Coldplay's career. I might also add that I grew up with that late 70's/early 80's punk/new wave generation, and so Coldplay reminds me of those good vibes. They are not alone, however, because you can point to The Killers, Bloc Party, Snow Patrol, Travis, The Futureheads, Keane, Interpol, White Lies, Stellastarr*, AWOLNATION, The Airborne Toxic Event, Cold War Kids, Blue October, Cut Copy, Florence + The Machine, Friendly Fires, Kaiser Chiefs, Matt & Kim, Mumford & Sons, Dan Black and others are working the same musical angle.<br /><br />This is to be celebrated. I felt the 1990's grunge movement was mostly dreadful. Whining, moaning, complaining--that was the alternative movement of the 1990's with a few exceptions. I see someone has felt the need to point out Pearl Jam having more integrity than Coldplay. Peal Jam is so musically separate from Coldplay, I don't even see the need for comparison. Within the genre, Coldplay do a fine job at what they do. I would never compare them to grunge music, heavy metal music, country music, reggae music or rap music--that's not what they're doing. No, what Coldplay are doing is making uplifting, melodic, alternative rock--like U2. Coldplay have yet to reinvent themselves like U2. But even, U2 came back to the music that made them popular in the first place eventually. To me, Coldplay has grown artistically as a band from album to album.<br /><br />I agree with some who say that \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" is the band's attempt to appeal to their fanbase by keeping the vibe from their previous two albums intact here. However, the band does push itself in other ways with the electronic hip-pop of \\"Princess Of China\\" with Rihanna adding just the right spine tingling vocals. If you had asked me back in 2005 would I picture Coldplay working perfectly with Rihanna I would have told you I don't see it happening. \\"Major Minus\\" is another track that has a more aggressive approach than what is expected from the band. It's more electric guitar heavy than most of what they have done in the past. The production from Brian Eno is also another shift in the band's overall sound. It still sounds very much like Coldplay, but the production appears more layered here, more produced. I like production values. I can't tell you the number of bland and boring acoustic sets I've heard from numerous artists throughout the past decade. I like the fact that Coldplay are experimenting with production values with Brian Eno. I like what Eno does, so I'm biased in that respect.<br /><br />There are a few tracks, ballads, that are certainly reminiscent of \\"The Scientist\\", \\"Yellow\\" and \\"Fix You\\" such as \\"Us Against The World\\" and \\"U.F.O.\\" There are also a handful of rocking uplifting \\"Beautiful Day\\" type tracks here like \\"Hurts Like Heaven\\", \\"Charlie Brown\\", \\"Every Teardrop Is A Waterfall\\" and \\"Don't Let It Break Your Heart\\".<br /><br />\\"Paradise\\" is also a bit of a departure for the band with its mid-tempo beat and swirling electronic bass textures and acoustic piano breaks.<br /><br />There are three short musical interlude segues that I felt (and I see others felt too) were unnecessary. Why didn't the band and Eno just make \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" part of \\"Hurts Like Heaven\\"? The same for \\"M.M.I.X.\\" with \\"Every Teardrop Is A Waterfall\\" and \\"A Hopeful Transmission\\" with \\"Don't Let It Break Your Heart\\". I admit I am not a fan of musical interludes/spoken interludes/segue ways. They distract from the overall listening experience for me. I really wish someone had told Eno and the band to please just make them part of the main tracks, do not break them up into separate songs.<br /><br />All this being said about \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" it easily makes my best of list for 2011. It has exactly what I am looking for: Strong, memorable melodies, uplifting choruses and verses, strong production values, and lyrics that are relatively accessible and appealing. It's the type of album I actually look forward to listening to repeatedly when so many albums I listen to just fall flat for one reason or another.<br /><br />Here is how \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" compares with the band's previous works:<br /><br />2000 Parachutes: Three Stars<br />2002 A Rush Of Blood To The Head: Three and a Half Stars<br />2005 X & Y: Four Stars<br />2008 Viva La Vida Or Death And All His Friends: Four and a Half Stars<br />2011 Mylo Xyloto: Four Starsues that I felt (and I see others felt too) were unnecessary. Why didn't the band and Eno just make \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" part of \\"Hurts Like Heaven\\"? The same for \\"M.M.I.X.\\" with \\"Every Teardrop Is A Waterfall\\" and \\"A Hopeful Transmission\\" with \\"Don't Let It Break Your Heart\\". I admit I am not a fan of musical interludes/spoken interludes/segue ways. They distract from the overall listening experience for me. I really wish someone had told Eno and the band to please just make them part of the main tracks, do not break them up into separate songs.  All this being said about \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" it easily makes my best of list for 2011. It has exactly what I am looking for: Strong, memorable melodies, uplifting choruses and verses, strong production values, and lyrics that are relatively accessible and appealing. It's the type of album I actually look forward to listening to repeatedly when so many albums I listen to just fall flat for one reason or another.  Here is how \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" compares with the band's previous works:  2000 Parachutes: Three Stars  2002 A Rush Of Blood To The Head: Three and a Half Stars  2005 X & Y: Four Stars  2008 Viva La Vida Or Death And All His Friends: Four and a Half Stars  2011 Mylo Xyloto: Four Stars

B005HNG1S0|2.0|I do not understand the raves this disc has been receiving.  To my ears the recorded sound is harsh, boxy, and although the winds sound excellent the strings sound steely and hard.  The piano sound is excessively reverberant and clangy.  As far as the playing goes, Ms. Grimaud simply does not understand Mozart's piano style; she is stiff in her phrasing even with her willful rubatos, pauses, and decrescendos at he end of phrases.  She lacks elegance, nuance, and dare I say it: beauty.  Perhaps this is due to the horrid piano sound captured by the DGG engineers.  As to her \\"controversial\\" playing of the Andante (Mozart's tempo marking) of K.488 in a Molto Adagio tempo, for me it simply does not work; the effect is not of greater profundity or emotion but rather sluggishness--the music seems to fall apart under her fingers.  Artur Schnabel made the same error in his recording of the concerto K.467, playing the Andante as Adagio--despite his gifts the music simply does not work at that tempo.  I am not reflexively opposed to fresh approaches to traditional playing of the classics, but it must serve the music, not distort it.  I have not heard Ms. Grimaud's other recordings, and based on this one, I have no wish to.  When it comes to Mozart I will turn to Schiff, Brendel, Goode, and Bilson.

0062112430|5.0|First Impressions: I read Delirium when it was first released back in January of this year, but I didn't have my blog then so I never wrote a review for it. I recently was given Pandemonium, the second book in the trilogy for review, so I thought it would finally get around to writing a review for Delirium. I did a quick re-read to freshen up my memory, not that I really needed to, but honestly I loved this book so I didn't mind! It was one of my favorite books I read this year, and before I go any further, if you haven't read it, you really need to because it is wonderful!!<br /><br />First 50 Pages: Delirium was one of the very first dystopian books that I read besides The Hunger Games and I think that it was the cause of my love for the genre. The first 50 pages of this book really hooked me right in both times I read it and I loved the setting and the premise of the story line. The world Oliver had created wasn't too different from the world we live in now, yet it is still unique. The author uses an amazing amount of detail and her imagery and world-building skills are fantastic. It was so easy to become completely absorbed into the main character, Lena's world. I love dystopians that doesn't seem far-fetched and it was easy for me to visualize most of the things in the story actually happening in real life. So, that is a major plus in my eyes.<br /><br />Characters & Plot: The basic story line revolves around Lena, who feels comfortable in her safe and secure bubble of a government-managed United States. She knows what to expect to happen in her future and everything will be chosen for her: her college, her career, and future husband, and even how many children they will one day have. Once Lena turns 18, she will have a procedure done to cure her of love. Yes, love. In this world, love is a disease and it is thought that it is the cause of just about everything that has ever gone wrong. All people are cured of the love disease, except for people called \\"invalids\\" or sympathizers of those \\"infected\\" with the love disease. Lena in the beginning of the story completely believes that love is exactly that, nothing more than a nasty disease that she cannot wait to be completely protected against and she even counts down the days to her own procedure. Lena's mother suffered and died because of the disease, and Lena's worst fear is of turning into her mother, who committed suicide.<br /><br />Then she meets Alex, a strange and kind of mysterious young man close to her own age 95 days before her procedure. In short, everything Lena thought she believed about the world she lived in, and even her own past, is thrown all out of wack. This book isn't fast-paced. It takes its good old time to get going, but I didn't mind because it left plenty of room for world-building and time to connect with each of the characters.<br /><br />I loved the transformation Lena takes in the book! She starts out as a character that really relies on everything being predictable, and she was predictable. She did exactly what she was told with no questions asked. She wasn't a risk taker and she knew, and accepted, what her future would be. She was like a caged animal, in fact, she pretty much was a caged animal. By the end of the book, she is anything but predictable. She takes chances. She has a chance at a real life and she makes her own choices. Were the choices she made the right choices? Well, I guess I will find out in the next book. This book ends with a major cliffhanger that left me screaming for more.<br /><br />I usually don't care for too much romance in books, but this time it was acceptable and expected deeming the circumstances. I loved the chemistry between Lena and Alex and the risks and lengths they take to stay together. It kind of reminded me of William Shakespeare's, \\"Romeo and Juliet.\\" In fact, the play was referred to quite a few times in the story, which I adored because it is one of my all-time favorites.<br /><br />Hana was also aninteresting character who makes a great transformation. She, unlike Lena, starts off as a bit of a wild-child. She takes the risks Lena had a hard time taking, and by the end of the book she turns out to be more like Lena was. She was only able to push herself to a certain limit, but she was content with what her future would be in the regulated world. I'm interested to see if she will continue to be on the same path in the next book.<br /><br />Final Thoughts: This book is absolutely incredible. I have nothing negative to say about Delirium, if anything, some people may find the pacing a tad slow but for me it was perfect. I have a feeling that Pandemonium is going to be more action packed because of how Delirium ended. I very, very highly recommend picking this book up! It is one that you won't want to miss!, unlike Lena, starts off as a bit of a wild-child. She takes the risks Lena had a hard time taking, and by the end of the book she turns out to be more like Lena was. She was only able to push herself to a certain limit, but she was content with what her future would be in the regulated world. I'm interested to see if she will continue to be on the same path in the next book.  Final Thoughts: This book is absolutely incredible. 0439023513|5.0|The conclusion to The Hunger Games trilogy is every bit as shocking and stunning as the first two books.  I've never described a book as a rollercoaster, but this one truly is. The entire trilogy is.  In this book, Katniss must come to terms with the last line of Catching Fire; \\"There is no District 12.\\"  This is the book where the rebellion finally hits high-gear. But as the book unfolds, Katniss begins to wonder if the alternative to the Capitol is much better.  Although some aspects of Collins' writing bothered me slightly- some things are glossed over a little conveniently glossed over, like how Katniss communicates with an Avox- a man who has had his tongue cut out, and she's often drugged and put to sleep when she's angry, or upset, and all that emotion is glossed over, and left for her to deal with a few days later, or when she comes around, the decisions she found difficult have been made.  Katniss is a slightly changed character, she almost seems to have PTSD in this book. It makes sense, when you think about what she's been through, but it's not the same person readers would have fallen in love with in The Hunger Games, but it's a logical progression for her character.  All that aside, Mockingjay is outstanding. It takes a slightly unexpected turn, but it all makes perfect sense given the events in the previous two books.   Any hope readers had throughout this book is stripped away by the time they're done. However, it's as grisly, nasty war, and nasty grisly deeds are part of that. It was always going to happen, after Katniss volunteered for Prim.  Yes, this book is dark, and grim, and relentless and nasty. So is war, which is the point Collins is making. Katniss exchanges one army for another, as it were, and finds herself being used in the same way, just by different people. The whole point is that the soldiers on either side of an army are essentially the same, and doesn't that make war pointless?  The choice we've all been waiting for her to make- thechoice between Peeta and Gale, is, as it turns out, almost inconsequential when compared to the choice she ends up making in the end.  I think a lot of people reading this book were hoping for a fairytale ending, and that's why they were disappointed. But it's war, few wars have happy endings.  I genuinely was nearly in tears at the end of this book. It's just brilliant.  For those of you who think this book is unsuitable for YA readers, have a little faith. So much YA fiction is trivial, as though all of life is rainbows and bunnies. I didn't read YA fiction when I was younger for that reason, it was unrealistic and soppy. Suzanne Collins writes it the way it should be. Realistic, and with the assumption that her teen readers are as demanding and intelligent as adult readers, which they are.

Some books are Fast Foods, quickly chewed and forgotten. Some are gourmet and need slow ingestion and deeper focus. 'Thinking..' belongs to the later category. Whatever you do, make sure you have time to reflect after each key chapters. Just close the eyes and run through a decision you made -- among get married; bought first house; had stress level up after first kid; never watched a documentary in last 2 years despite having a lot in Netflix queue; have lesser faith on voters of Alaska, or Hawaii etc. -- and a lot of worthy self-introspection will ensue.  Metaphorically speaking, Kahneman's model breaks the 'logical brain' (not the hardware) into two components - fast (System I) and slow (System II). Think of System I being Dr. Watson - quickly arriving at decisions, not always accurate, neither insightful; while Sherlock Holmes (System II) is lazier and does not want to deal with every single question as producing an answer is taxing to the detective. System I is shallow, but extremely fast in processing because if follows some models and heuristics. When the models do not work as intended, go against -- say, probabilistic answer of whether a brief 3-line narrative about a person maps to a lawyer or artist -- it often creates 'fallacy'. When the models always produce a certain output from a given input set, the process is often an example of 'bias'.   e.g., A vaccine would save 90% impacted vs. it would kill 10 - may produce different emotional outcome even though both inputs are logically analogous. Similarly, while describing a person starting with positive keywords and ending with negative ones will have highly variable impact than starting with the negative keywords, even though in both sets we use the same keywords.   Most of these biases, heuristics and fallacies are fairly well known thanks to the slew of behavioral economics books published in last 4 years (Predictably Irrational; Nudge; Blink; Being Wrong; How We Decide; You are Not So Smart etc). What set Kahneman apart from the other books are (a) genesis thinking - he and co-researcher Amos Tversky are practically founding fathers of this science, and (b) an exceptionally lucid narrative expressed with humility that reaches out to the opposite side of opinion and try to honestly reconcile. e.g., Kahneman carefully calls out the difference between an expert's fast thinking that is honed with years of experience (a fire-fighter getting out of a house without knowing why; a doctor diagnosing a disease by looking at patient's skin tone etc) and the usual person's day-to-day decisions, especially inter-personal or financial ones. While doing so he also establishes a strong case against the \\"econs\\" that consider humans a perfectly rational agents while taking financial decisions.   The second half of the book is a mind candy as Kahneman delves deeper into 'Prospect Theory' and establishes a Profit-Loss-Risk Aversion-Risk Aggression quadrant. This is especially important segment for young people in today's tough economy.  Very rarely we do get a chance to transform ourselves. Most of the times the transformation happens through up-leveling our thinking and a mentor showing us a completely new plateau we were not even aware of. 'Thinking, Fast and Slow' does precisely that. Buy this book and gift to people you work with, you are in a relationship with or people who you think 'do not understand you' or vice versa.   By all means, if you are serious about introspection not reading this book would almost tantamount to a petty crime.

0865478570|5.0|David Bellos' book on translation is a little modern masterpiece. By turns witty, funny, introspective, erudite and common, this book defies simple qualification. It's about translation, yes, but it's also about much more than that. How did language first come about? What is 'language' anyway? Why do different people speak different languages? And where does translation fit into this? And speaking of translation: what can it do, and what can't it do? Why don't we all just have little Hitchhiker's Guide fishies in our ears? Failing that, how about machines or computers - can't they just do the work of all those professionals we call 'translators'?<br /><br />Bellos artfully and sensibly navigates his way through these questions. This is a well-read and wide-ranging tome. The reader is transported from Plato to the Roman Empire to Louis XIV's France and to Ottoman Istanbul; from the Nuremberg Trials to the world of modern-day literature.<br /><br />And it's quite a revolutionary journey. In poppy, understated prose Bellos claims that language is not a vehicle for the transmission of one's own thoughts into another's mind. Rather, it's a social construct: a messy, confusing and wonderful way of saying \\"I am not you, but me.\\" And translation? Translation allows one group to share its experiences with another. They don't share abstract antiseptic ideals in a Platonic sense, but rather ill-defined 'meaning.' Cooing a baby, touching another's arm and saying 'The pen is in the box' are all analogous acts. They are at once social and abstract, at once thoughts and feelings and meanings. Translation allows us to make sense of this fizzy abstraction of socio-language. If, after all, something can be translated, then surely it has some meaning. For what is translation if not the transfer of meaning?<br /><br />Is That A Fish in Your Ear poignantly and unpresumptuously speaks to these issues. It's a wide-ranging little masterpiece. Particularly recommended are the last 15 or so pages of the book, Bellos' so-titled 'Afterbabble.' If you've no time for anything else, borrow the book from a friend and read the Afterbabble. You won't be missing mental food for weeks afterward.titled 'Afterbabble.' If you've no time for anything else, borrow the book from a friend and read the Afterbabble. You won't be missing mental food for weeks afterward.

B005C7MVC6|4.0|Bon Iver is the newest record by singer-songwriter Justin Vernon. It was released June 21st here in the United States and slightly earlier worldwide. It has received rave reviews for the most part and has gotten an 86 out of 100 cumulative score on Metacritic.com. The 10-song album was written and produced by Vernon himself and is the culmination of a long period of success since 2007 in the wake of the mononucleosic tear jerker and critical knockout, For Emma Forever Ago.    In 2006, Justin Vernon retreated to his father's cabin in northwestern Wisconsin while sick with mono and disillusioned by the breakup of his band. There, armed with primitive recording gear, he began recording songs as they came to him. He had not intended to record an entire record but luckily for us he did because the result was one of the most achingly beautiful pieces of art in years.  In 2007, the world got its first helping of Justin Vernon's solo project, Bon Iver, with the self released album For Emma Forever Ago. Playing with themes of isolation and depression, the album gave us an intimate, if not fleeting, glimpse into the gloomy mind of the artist and flew in the face of big record labels with its huge success despite low budget production. Well, this raised a few eyebrows and opened a few pocket books and, as a result, Bon Iver was signed to the hugely popular indie labels Jagjaguwar and 4AD. With a new budget, it was time for a follow-up.    It came in the form of the eponymous Bon Iver. Bon Iver kicks off with \\"Perth,\\" a track that plays like a summary of every idea introduced in Emma. A woozy guitar riff gives way to Vernon's instantly recognizable falsetto, setting any sophomore slump notions to rest and perfectly setting the tone for the rest of the record.   Tracks like the swooning \\"Towers\\" lay to rest any preconceived notions of being self-referential by introducing church cadences and chirping synths previously unheard in Vernon's compositions. This time around, Vernon seems focused, as if he has awoken from his dreamy delirium ready to show us he's more than just a fluke.    With all its overarching beauty and confidence, Bon Iver is not without its problems. While the record retains much of its predecessor's moodiness and tone in terms of lyrics and subject matter, it does not retain its sense of intimacy. Gone are the cheap buzzing microphones and humming desktop computers that gave the first full-length album its timeless feel. This is forgivable though. If artists didn't grow and change, we would have missed out on hundreds of amazing albums (e.g. Bob Dylan going electric or Radiohead's Kid A).    Bon Iver is a powerful and convincing sophomore effort that is worthy of your time. It is a record that rewards patience and demands attention. Although it doesn't retain all of the same qualities that we fell in love with in certain other previous efforts, it does introduce enough new ideas to more than make up for its omissions.
B004M3H17Q|3.0|I immediately took issue with the album for the simple fact that the first couple of minutes of a few songs sounded very similar in terms of vocal style with a musician that I do happen to really like, Justin Vernon from Bon Iver fame. However, I still wasn't completely in love with the vocals as they sounded like a blatant rip-off of another vocalist I am a fan of, Antony Hegarty of Antony and the Johnsons.  But aside from this small disappointment, I decided to actually give the album a fighting chance and listen to it in its entirety. The album gets off to a nice and interesting start with \\"Unluck,\\" a simple, melodic, piano-driven song with fairly simple vocals. There is the occasional break in the monotony with a strange, record-scratching effect interrupting every five seconds or so. And then the beat in the background comes more into the forefront, building and building, and it jumps back to the original pattern at the beginning and more effective, Auto-Tuned vocals appear.   The fourth and fifth tracks (\\"Lindisfarne I\\" and \\"Lindisfarne II\\") basically sounds like Blake was shamelessly copying tracks that he probably listened to repeatedly from Bon Iver's For Emma Forever Ago release. The only noticeable difference was that Blake used even more voice-effecting computer techniques for his vocals, to which I would say the popular quote \\"Too much of a good thing can sometimes be bad.\\"  One of the few all-around great tracks on the record is the Feist cover \\"Limit To Your Love.\\" The cover is actually done very well and one of the few instances where Blake's delicate, vocals seem to work to his advantage. It could easily compete with the original version and that is saying a lot because I am a huge fan of Feist. And again, Blake's brilliant producer skills come into play and lend a special touch to the original version. I never thought a hip-hop beat could make this Feist classic sound even better.  I think that Blake's self-titled debut is at least worth a listen for his talent as a producer alone. He, however, has a long way to go as far as learning to find his own distinctive style as a vocalist and as a songwriter. Maybe this is just the case of a brilliant producer who wants to do too much. Maybe he would be better off producing for artists who have that extra something that makes them \\"them\\". Or maybe Blake just needs a little more time to come into his own.

1451648537|4.0|\\"I wanted my kids to know me.  I wasn't always there for them, and I wanted them to know why and to understand what I did. So I wanted to make sure someone heard what I had to say.\\" - Steve Jobs reason for having Walter Isaacson write his biography<br /><br />I did not intend to read this book.<br /><br />I did not know Steve Jobs, but I know several people who did, including a few who worked with him.  Most described the experience of working with him as 1) priceless, and 2) one that no amount of money could get them to repeat.  I have heard many stories of his brilliance and brutality.  I, have read several books about Apple (the early years and after Jobs returned), and watched an excellent documentary on Pixar.  And I don't usually read biographies.<br /><br />The fact that I did read the book is testimony to the genius of Steve Jobs (and Jeff Bezos) and the talent of Walter Isaacson.  I was in a hotel room one evening having trouble falling asleep, and downloaded the first few chapters of the Kindle edition (for free) to my iPad.  No one has approached Jobs' genius for creating an immediate, exhilarating user experience in the digital age, and it's hard to imagine he could have chosen a better biographer than Isaacson.  Within fifteen minutes, I was hooked.<br /><br />Isaacson clearly did his research, and the fact that Jobs gave him complete access to his life, and encouraged friends and foes (in Jobs life, many people are both) to speak honestly about their relationships and experiences with Jobs gave him the detail he needed.  But his talent allows him to tell a richly textured, deeply affecting story from cover-to-cover (undoubtedly, an antiquated expression for a digital book).<br />As a father of an adopted son, I was especially moved by the description of an incident when Jobs, at six or seven years old, told a neighborhood girl he was adopted, to which she responded, \\"So does that mean your real parents didn't want you?\\"<br /><br />\\"I remember running into the house, crying.  And my parents said, `No, you have to understand.'  They were very serious and looked me straight in the eye.  They said, `We specifically picked you out.'  Both of my parents said that and repeated it slowly for me.  And they put an emphasis on every word in that sentence.\\"<br /><br />Throughout the book, Isaacson reports similar details that give the reader keen insights into the events that shaped Jobs, without injecting amateur psychological suppositions.  The result is great reporting delivered in a wonderful narrative.  As I read the book, I realized that my image of Jobs, while accurate, was a caricature.  By the end of the book, I had a fully formed picture of a brilliant, passionate, and deeply flawed human being.<br /><br />Steve Jobs never read any part of the biography.  He gave Walter Isaacson complete control of the story, although, after seeing the initial design for the book cover, he threatened to kill the project, until he received complete creative control of the cover design.  Initially, I was amazed that someone who had guarded his privacy so closely would relinquish control of his life story.  But then I realized that he simply followed the process he had used in business.  Jobs did not personally create technology, films, commercials, etc.  But he carefully selected incredibly talented people, provided them the resources to create \\"insanely great\\" products, and exerted absolute control over product packaging.  Steve Jobs, the book, is not insanely great, but is an informative, interesting, and worthy result of his and Walter Isaacson's creative process.e responded, \\"So does that mean your real parents didn't want you?\\"   \\"I remember running into the house, crying. 1451627289|5.0|I'm one of King's \\"constant readers\\". Having come of age just as King was first topping the best seller charts, I've read most of what he has written. And while I'm still sure to buy and read his latest, I'm not as sure to love it as I once was. I loved 11/22/63.  For me, King's books are never about the horror, the gore, or the supernatural. Sure, I willingly suspend disbelief to enjoy them; but that isn't WHY I enjoy them. The reason I love King's books are two-fold: He writes some of the most loveable and fully fleshed out characters of any author, and he is a pure story teller. There is not a lot of literary pretense, just a lot of manic page turning.  11/22/63 is no exception. For a few days I hated all aspects of my life that didn't permit me time to read. I didn't want to work, sleep, or interact with my family. I just wanted to follow the exploits of Jake Epping in the mid-twentieth century. And when I finished, I didn't know how to feel. I was exhilarated to have just read such a wonderful book; but I mourned the fact that it was over.  The thing I was most pleased about was that with 11/22/63 King didn't fall into a trap that he often lands in. In my opinion, for the last 20 years or so King has had difficulty ending his books. The buildup is usually wonderful, the characters fully alive in my imagination, and then he realizes that somehow the story must end. Sooooo, the plants come alive, or giant insects invade the story, or something else just as implausible and unsatisfying. Not so in 11/22/63. The conclusion is just as enjoyable as the rest of the book. The only letdown is that it's time to put the book on the shelf, and try to find something else to read.  For anyone who has been mildly disappointed by the books of middle aged King, and who long for his vintage books: read 11/22/63. It is pure pleasure!

1594202842|4.0|I happened to see this book in the ebook listing of my public library's catalog, and having read all the media hype about it, decided to put my name on the waiting list. I was fully prepared to hate this woman after all I had read about her. I was pleasantly surprised at my reaction to the book. It was written in a self-deprecating tongue-in-cheek manner rather than with a tone of superiority. The author was committed to what she describes as the Chinese method of parenting (or rather, her interpretation of it), which worked out fine for her older daughter, but not so well for the younger one. In fact, she was forced to modify her harsh approach with the younger one and give her some space.   In analyzing what she describes as the difference between Chinese and Western parenting, she makes some valid observations, but her approach is extreme. While she claims to have done all in the name of her children, to me what was most telling was reading the notes she left for her daughter Lulu, which she had inadvertently forgotten to put away while entertaining some of her Yale students in her daughter's practice room. The depth of research she put into her daughter's music was over the top and an example of extreme micromanaging. It is one thing to shepherd your children to music lessons and to make them practice, but the work involved in understanding the music and then giving specific advice as to how to execute the various sections of the composition made me wonder who she was doing it for. She documents her children's outbursts, but doesn't seem to address the accusation that she might be the frustrated musician, and maybe she would have been better off taking lessons herself and having her own creative outlet rather than foisting it upon her kids.  Still, she recognizes some of the shortcomings of her approach, but in the end, although she had to loosen the reins on her younger daughter, she is unapologetic. She even feels that her heavy-handed approach gave the rebellious daughter the tools to follow her own passion (tennis). In all, it was a thought-provoking memoir, designed to shock, but it did raise some valid issues regarding parenting styles.

1594484805|5.0|Drive is about \\"the 3rd drive\\" which relates to people's intrinsic motivation (rather than basic motivation and extrinsic motivation). It is a book that exposes the research data related to intrinsic motivation and tries to convince the reader that there is \\"something wrong\\" with most traditional management theories related to motivation. (and he does a good job convincing).<br /><br />The book is an easy read and written a little bit too popular. (this was pointed out very well by the great Amazon review of Walter Bock). Still it is worth a read. The book is structured in 2 parts and a bunch of appendixes. Part 1 talks about the difference between intrinsic and extrinsic rewards and why the external rewards don't work (at least, don't work in a modern knowledge-worker setting). The second part then answers the question: \\"If extrinsic motivation doesn't work, how can we create an environment in which people are motivated\\".<br /><br />Dan Pink's answer: 3 elements are important for intrinsic motivation:<br />- Mastery<br />- Self-direction<br />- Purpose<br /><br />Mastery is about getting good at something. Self-direction is about being in control of your own life and decisions. Purpose is about trying to achieve something meaningful. (these 3 points are very similar to the work of Richard Hackman, which was referred at times).<br /><br />The book is a very easy read, well written. It contains interesting stories and facts and does a great job at convincing the reader. This book is important as extrinsic rewards are still everywhere and are destroying the motivation to do work. It will take a long time to change that, but this book is one step in the right direction. An important book and thus, even with its drawbacks, still 5 stars.

0385534639|5.0|The Night Circus follows two groups of people in two timelines that eventually converge. The first plot surrounds Celia and Marco, two brilliant young magicians who have been pitted against each other by their masters in a complex game, with the Night Circus as its venue, that even the two competitors don't understand the rules of.  The second plot follows Bailey, a young man who dreads becoming a farmer like his father and finds his only solace in the Night Circus when it comes to town.  I can see why this book creates such a divided reaction -- the plot moves along languidly, dropping small hints and revealing clues slowly as we spend much of 300 pages just getting to know and understand the world of The Night Circus, as well as its characters, and then racing along for the last 60 pages or so.  This is one of those books that could not only divide people, but a person reading it at the right or wrong time might love it or hate it.  Personally, I loved it. I enjoyed feeling almost like one of the Night Circus patrons, moving in awe from tent to tent, wanting to explore each square inch of the circus grounds.  Morgenstern creates magical-realism at its finest, blending the mundane and the fantastic, and leaving some of the questions about how it works unanswered, to keep that edge of mysteriousness.  I will say I found a few things at the climax to be a tad deus ex machina-y, and I feel it ended somewhat abruptly. (Personally, I would have been happy if it hadn't ended at all.)  Overall though, I thought it was a great book that really captured my imagination.
0240810961|5.0|The title of the review states it,  this is the best book on drawing I have ever read.  The book is a bunch of short chapters further grouped by an overall topic.  It is the first of a two book series.  The editor of the book took lectures by Walt Stanchfield (a Disney animator) and put them together in book format.  Because of the shortness of chapters, it is easy to pick and put down the book after a short reading (like reading magazines) and not feel like you have to read it all the way through.  I also think you can put what you learn in just one chapter to use right away without having to read further.    Though the lectures are geared towards animators, anyone drawing (at any skill level) will learn from this book.  I think my drawings (which have nothing to do with animation) have improved since reading this book.  And I have a better idea of how to approach my drawings instead of looking at a blank sheet of paper and thinking \\"ok what now\\".  What you will not learn is mechanics.  You will not learn how to put pencil or pen to paper (though there was one chapter on holding the pencil loser).  You will not learn anatomy.  You will not learn the correct way to draw a hand or face or where to place subjects within a drawing.  There are plenty of books on mechanics.  This book is about taking what mechanics you have and making your drawings better.  What you will learn is how to make your drawing more interesting.  How to capture the essence of what you are trying to draw.  How to get more realism (not realism from a physical sense - wow that is photographic - but realism in the action sense - yes that is what it woul look like if someone was holding a box and trying to lift it).  This is done through a series of text and example sketches (some from the lectures which are very sketchy and some from Disney animation).  About the only thing I would have liked to have known is how large the originals were.  I like to compare my drawings to what I am learning and oneof the things I like to know is time it took to draw and size.  I highly recommend this book and I am anxious to read the next.

0756404738|5.0|Overall: 9.5/10  A Wise Man's Fear isn't quite as easy to get pulled into as The Name of the Wind.  All of the same qualities are present in the sequel to make it a great book, but everything feels a little bit more forced than the first.  This isn't to say that it is bad, but A Wise Man's Fear doesn't pull you into Kvothe's world quite as completely as its predecessor until more than halfway through the book.  However, there are a great many contributing factors.  First of all, The Wise Man's Fear is about half again as long as The Name of the Wind, which means that it necessarily moves a little bit more slowly.  Add to this the fact that the University, fresh and new when Kvothe arrives in the first book, is now a little old hat, and you have a necessary start to the book that isn't as exciting as it could be.  This combined with a large number of intermissions between the beginning and middle that pull us out of Kvothe's story and into the present day lead to a slightly boring and somewhat disjointed start to the second book.  However, keep reading because, the second half of the book is stellar.  Writing: 10/10  The listening experience doesn't changed significantly from the first to second book.  The writing still flows smoothly, and the reader does an excellent job, the primary difficulty in the first half of the book is chapter organization and subject matter.  However, much of what is somewhat boring in the first half is necessary for later in the story, so it's worth the effort to read.  Characters: 9.5/10  Again, Rothfuss leads us deep into Kvothe's mind and personal worldview.  Kvothe is still an excellent character, but the secondary characters in The Wise Man's Fear are much more real than those of the first book.  Pella, Simmon, and even Devi are more fully developed, and new secondary characters like the Maer Alvaron, Tempi, and Vashet are also very well developed.  These strong secondary characters allow Kvothe to feel even more real by comparison, which leads to a more strongly character driven book.  World: 10/10  Again Rothfuss' world is excellently developed, from the forests Vint, to Felurian's twilight glade, to the windy hills of Ademre Rothfuss paints a picture of his world that is both believable and beautiful.  He includes a vast amount of detail that will keep world lovers dedicated for ages.  This is still where his writing shines the most, and I have not doubt that it will continue to do so.  Plot: 10/10  The plot of Kvothe's life takes an interesting turn in this book.  I won't say much, because I don't want to ruin it for anyone, but if you are a discerning reader then you'll probably catch the gist of it by the end of the first book, although I must admit that the execution of this twist is wonderful.  Pacing: 7/10  As I mentioned above, The Wise Man's Fear has some pacing issues.  These aren't difficult to overcome, the story is still interesting and the writing is still good, but almost the first half of the book left me with the feeling that Rothfuss was simply waiting until he could start the second half.  He fills the space with interesting stories, and necessary events to get from place to place, but it leaves the reader with the feeling that something is missing.  Commentary: 8.0/10  Rothfuss' commentary in The Wise Man's Fear was more blatant that in The Name of the Wind.  However, it reminded me of an extended version of Heinlein's Stranger in a Strange Land, with different ideas presented through cultural conflict.  Kvothe travels from the University to Severen (in Vint) and then to Felurian's glade and then to Ademre, before returning to Severen and the University.  In his travels Kvothe encouters several different cultures, and much of the commentary in the book is presented in the form of conflicting ideas in and between these cultures, and Kvothe's reactions.  While very different than the commentary in The Name of the Wind, the commentary in The Wise Man's Fear is about equally well presented, with some ideas presented more strongly or completely than others.  However, like Stranger in a Strange Land, I do not agree with a lot of the commentary that Rothfuss make in this book.  Conclusion:  The Wise Man's Fear is definitely worth reader, especially if you like The Name of the Wind, but don't expect it to be the same book - of course, that would be boring.

From the action scenes to the casting, the film does its job of being a solid action movie. The greatest strength of this film is Jason Momoa. He is Conan. He has the physique of an 80's action hero (think of Stallone or Schwarzenegger), but the personality of a modern anti-hero. The question you may be asking is if he's better than Schwarzenegger's Conan? The answer is based solely on personal preference. Both of them play Conan equally well, but their different. Schwarzenegger's Conan was stoic and cold. Momoa's Conan is primal and purely instinctual. Again, I love both, and I think Momoa makes an amazing Conan.   Stephen Lang's Khalar Zym, was nowhere near as amazing as James Earl Jones' Thulsa Doom. But you could tell Lang had fun playing Zym. And when an actor has fun playing a villain, especially one that is as clichÃ©d and cartoony as Zym, you can't help but be sucked in. And I fell for Lang's Zym.   There are problems with this movie. There is no denying that. But every problem I have lies with Marcus Nispel's direction and the film's three different writers. I think the choices Nispel made during filming and post-production weren't acceptable. And the script, after three different writers, still needed polishing. But by sheer luck, Conan the Barbarian is the director's best film to date, due to his star's Momoa and Lang. They carry this movie into B movie (or passable) territory that hopefully will find an audience.   There are plenty out there who will disagree with me liking this film, but I say give the film a chance. It's not for everyone. Conan the Barbarian isn't better than Schwarzenegger's classic, but it's not worse either. It's a good action film, with a good cast, and great set pieces. One thing I should mention for Blu Ray fans is this Blu Ray disc is reference quality. The picture and sound are demo worthy. As for the 3D quality... it's not horrible, but there are better 3D Blu Rays out there. It's average. There's some depth, and some pop out moments, but overall, it doesn't compare to Avatar or Beauty and the Beast 3D Blu Rays. For the price though, if you're a fan of the film, or end up liking it, it's worth buying.

038572179X|5.0|It's not often a book can rank on Time's list of 100 Greatest Novels of All Time and still be a good leisure read, at least for a seventeen-year-old.  However, Atonement's riveting and unique plotline set in the fascinating world of war-ravaged England and France kept me in eager anticipation from page one.    Ian McEwan has a way of writing that brings out the beauty of simple objects and events, such as the broken vase that came to represent the relationship between Robbie and Cecilia: not much at first glance, but containing too much sentimentality to let go.  McEwan lays down the facts in exquisite detail but offers minimal emotional pull, forcing the reader to create their own and stretch their imagination.  On multiple occasions, I found myself watching the scene unfold instead of just reading about it.  For example, the scene involving the RAF officer at Dunkirk actually made me pity the officer, when I wasn't focused on my anger towards the soldiers.  Getting emotional about a book was a first for me.    The book in general is captivating, as it takes a twist to the typical war-time love story.  Instead of focusing on the couple, the story follows the regret of the girl who separated them.  The separate parts allow readers to understand the different sides of the same story and set the playing field for dramatic irony.    Unlike the movie, which has a simpler job in creating foreshadowing, the book keeps you guessing until the end as to who really committed the crime by choosing to point the finger at a minor character that the reader never meets.  I won't spoil the end, but it has a Shutter Island-esque effect that makes one wonder how much was a lie.  Some readers may be dissatisfied that the story didn't go their way, but I personally appreciated McEwan's dedication to telling a believable, individual story.  The multiple surprises tucked into the last half of part three and the epilogue, combined with McEwan's flowing, descriptive paragraphs, will makethis book one of the most memorable ones on my shelf, and I look forward to rediscovering it in coming years.
B001PB3RU8|5.0|This album opens with the line \\"Happiness hit her like a train on a track,\\" which reflects the tone of everything that comes after. Everything, even pleasure and love, comes out of Florence Welch's artistry like a fist in the gut, unexpected and brutal. She grabs bluesy traditions in songs like \\"Hurricane Drunk\\" and \\"Kiss With a Fist,\\" then hit a lush prog rock tone in \\"Rabbit Heart,\\" keeping us constantly on our toes.  Florence's use of retro traditions amid contemporary sounds reminds me of acts like Gomez or Paloma Faith. Like those acts, this album has an unmistakable European club vibe, but bolsters that sound with an intensely American feel. But where Gomez channels blues and Faith contemporizes club jazz, Florence uses a much wider swath of American sound. Almost no sound (except country) goes untapped.  Additionally, my honey points out a certain similarity to Bjork. Like the Icelandic Golden Throat, Florence creates an intricate soundscape that equally rewards fans for frenzied dancing and more restrained thoughts about the lyrics. In fact, I would consider Florence more successful than Bjork, because her dissonances are more subtle, sneaking past your defenses and surprising you with sudden insight.  If I had to find one fault, I'd say that I wish every single song didn't have to reach for the stars. Pretty much every track is an elaborate showstopper that tries to rock your socks off. Sadly, this leaves you with little opportunity to catch your breath, and the songs start to blend together. Just one or two slower-paced tracks could have taken this album from merely exceptional and into the ranks of near-perfection.  In a pop landscape where nearly every hitmaker sounds exactly the same as every other, Florence Welch taps others' songbooks while still sounding uniquely herself. Her lush songs, many of which conceal brutal themes in excitingly danceable tunes, reward multiple listenings. I wish more acts of this caliber came out of the major labels today. And I'm thankful I found this one when I did.

0670022950|4.0|How does this sound for entertainment: Your date asks you out to the theater to watch a live cat slowly lowered into a fire and burned to death, howling with pain as it is singed, roasted, and finally carbonized?<br /><br />If that isn't your idea of entertainment, don't hop into the next time machine heading back to medieval Europe.<br /><br />In 16th-century Paris, throngs - including kings and queens -- flocked to watch such gruesome spectacles, shrieking with laughter as cats and other animals were tortured to death on stage.<br /><br />Torture and violence were woven into the fabric of life, from the sexualized sadism of London, where elaborately designed and decorated torture devices were the pinnacle of artistic creativity, to the widespread practice of hacking off the nose of anyone who spited you (the source of the strange idiom, \\"to cut off your nose to spite your face\\").<br /><br />In contrast, whether we know it or not, we are now enjoying the most peaceful period in all of human history. Indeed, the precipitous decline in violence of all types may be \\"the most significant and least appreciated development in the history of our species,\\" argues Steven Pinker, a renowned professor of psychology at Harvard University, in an epic tome, The Better Angels of Our Nature: Why Violence Has Declined.<br /><br />Given the tenor of the daily news headlines, Pinker knows his claim sounds far-fetched. But in 800 pages of research and analysis, augmented by hundreds of charts and tables, he convincingly establishes that violence is indeed heading in one direction: down.<br /><br />The decline is drastic across-the-board, in both state-sanctioned and individual violence: International wars, civil wars, terrorism (an obsession far out of proportion to its prevalence), slavery, sexual violence, child abuse, infanticide.<br /><br />Key to the decline, Pinker argues, is the diminishing currency of both tribalism and faith-based dogma, with its belief that suffering will be rewarded in the after-life and its press to defend unverifiable tenets with violence.<br /><br />Our ancestors were a \\"morally retarded\\" bunch, he argues, who couldn't see past their own noses - that is, if they still had them - to consider the perspectives of others.<br /><br />A combination of scientific rationality, literacy, mobility, urbanization, and technology has enabled humankind to become more self-reflective and to feel sympathy for others - from racial and ethnic minorities to women to homosexuals to children and even to animals.<br /><br />In his zeal to share his glad tidings, Pinker has an annoying habit of glossing over current problems. He pays scant attention to the United States's current practice of endless war, or the escalating costs of military engagement. He claims that the political disenfranchisement of African Americans has been remedied, ignoring the fact that millions of Black men in America are systematically denied the right to vote due to felony convictions. Similarly, he trivializes antigay violence, relying on FBI data that drastically underestimates its prevalence. It's hard to be too thrilled when inner-city children are still sleeping in bathtubs to avoid being killed by stray bullets. At times, he seems overly enamored of his own brilliance, rambling on and becoming repetitious.<br /><br />Still, the central theme is crucially important. Despite all of the world's problems, the dramatic decline of violence, notes Pinker, \\"is an accomplishment we can savor, and an impetus to cherish the forces of civilization and enlightenment that made it possible.\\"enets with violence. But, herein lies the beauty of these movies: every time I watch them, it's like I'm unfolding a new layer. While I was disappointed with Reloaded and Revolutions when they first came out, as I revisit them I am beginning to appreciate what the Wachowski Brothers created. Sure, they may not be as refreshing as the first movie, but viewed as a whole they weave a story that is both compelling and gripping at the same time. It's the story of a savior beginning to understand his powers, then sacrificing himself for his people. It is true that these movies will probably not reach the same status as other trilogies like Star Wars, or Lord of the Rings, but I believe that these movies truly define a generation. This set does the series justice. In it you will find all 3 movies plus the Animatrix in full gorgeous HD, a slew of special features exploring the philosophical implications of these movies, and a slew of documentaries exploring the production of all three movies. Definitely a set worth owning, and a series worth revisiting.

B004LR5K0I|2.0|Walk A Mile In My Shoes: The Essential 70's Masters includes 120 of Elvis Presley's 1970's songs. However only 11 of these songs are truly outstanding. Outstanding songs include The Wonder Of You, Burning Love, It's A Matter Of Time, Separate Ways, I Washed My Hands In Muddy Water, Whole Lot-ta Shakin' Going On, Tomorrow Never Comes, Make the World Go Away, Funny How Time Slips Away, Merry Christmas Baby, and You Asked Me To. In addition, there are four other notable songs, which I will name in this review. The Elvis Presley of the 1950's and 1960's was the King of rock and roll, rhythm and blues, and slow romantic ballads. However, the Elvis Presley of the 1970's is a different singer ; he is an artist that sings mostly popular songs first recorded by others, country western music, and heavily orchestrated live performances. Almost completely gone is the rock and roll Elvis; all that remains are Burning Love and Whole Lot-ta Shakin' Going On. Almost completely gone is the rhythm and blues Elvis; all that remains are I Washed My Hands In Muddy Water and Merry Christmas Baby. Almost completely gone is the slow romantic ballads Elvis; all that remains is the beautiful The Wonder Of You. The Elvis of the 1970's interprets other artists material; examples include James Taylor's Steamroller Blues, Bob Dylan's Don't Think Twice It's All Right, Eddy Arnold's Make The World Go Away, Frank Sinatra's My Way, and Waylon Jennings' You Asked Me To. The Elvis of the 1970's rarely records in the studio. He either refuses studio recording or abruptly cancels scheduled studio sessions at the very last moment. The Elvis of The 1970's performs live in concert, where the accoustics and live recording equipment make Elvis' live performances clearly inferior to songs that are recorded in the studio. As a result, even a beautiful song like The Impossible Dream would have sounded so much better if Elvis had recorded it in the studio. Compared to Elvis Presley's 1950's and 1960's songs, Walk A Mile in My Shoes; The Essential 70's material is a huge disappointment: 105 of the 120 songs are not even notable! Eighty-seven percent of the music found on Essential 70's Masters is either poor or only  satisfactory and seems to drift by without exciting or captivating the listener. I fairly award the music found on the Essential 70's Masters a three star rating. The Elvis Presley of the 1970's was not the King of any type of popular music! Ten years of making mostly mundane movies, serious and escalating prescription drug abuse, Priscilla's divorce, aimless living, and regular concert touring took a very heavy toll upon the musical achievements of the 1970's Elvis Presley. Fortunately for me, I am a devoted fan of Elvis' outstanding music, a serious collector of 87 of  Elvis Presley's cds, and I already own copies of the 11 outstanding songs that are found on Essential 70's Masters. Walk A Mile In My Shoes: Essential 70's Masters is entirely expendable for the serious collector of Elvis Presley's music! I am going to sell these five cds. This sale tells me that I already own enough of Elvis Presley's music to fully appreciate his musical legacy. The packaging of this five cd set is the worst cd packaging that I have ever encountered! All five cds are jammed into a small box in a way that made it extremely difficult to remove the five cds without bending or cracking them. The box does not protect the five cds, so one has to use one's own cd cases to store them. I award the packaging of this five cd set a one star rating. Taking into account both the music and the packaging, I award Walk A Mile In My Shoes: Essential 70's Masters a two star rating. If you are a serious collector of Elvis Presley's music, I strongly recommend that you NOT PURCHASE Walk A Mile In My Shoes: Essential 70's Masters.  John

0061969656|3.0|This is my first Cornwell book. As much as I enjoyed it, I'm sure it won't be my last.  At first I thought his opening, \\"Every day is ordinary, until it isn't,\\" was hokey. And the smaltzy dialogue of the first couple pages made me wonder if I really wanted to continue. Fortunately I did. This is carefully plotted and the characters are mostly quite believable. There is a pair of twins that almost make me cringe, but the plot moves quickly enough and there are enough clever little twists to keep me very entertained.  The immediacy of first person narration was a good idea. It also lets the author off the hook for complete historical accuracy. Hey, even history books have enough inaccuracies to sometimes qualify as fiction. The narrator, though, is cocky and arrogant, proud of his paganism, and still likable.   What I liked most about this book is the attention given to details regarding setting, action and realistic human motivations. Cornwell makes the scene come alive for the reader as if he has been describing events in real time.  There were a couple things I didn't like. One is that the antagonists have no real presence in this book. They're the Danes, of course, but they're always at a distance and never up close with faces we can visualize. They lack depth.  Our hero always thinks faster than anyone around him. Since there's seemingly nothing he can't do, he seems unrealistically invincible. His antipathy to Christianity wears thin after a while and it goes beyond mere characterization and doesn't contribute to the plot since (in the first half of the book, at least) it becomes quite heavy handed. The same goes for the monks who \\"sing like little birdies\\"--meant as caricature? What's the point?  Ultimately, I was left with the impression that Cornwell is a natural storyteller with a very keen eye for detail and invention, but here he probably got a little careless in places.  One thing I'd prefer to see here is a much more detailed map of England.

B005M68S4W|2.0|For the sake of balance and perspective, I'm giving this a lowly two stars.  It may possibly be worth three, but surely not the uncritical five automatically awarded by most of Mr Bennett's slavishly loyal fans.  First up, most of the heavy lifting on this album is done by the guests, not by Mr Bennett.  k.d. lang, Josh Groban, Norah Jones, Faith Hill and Mariah Carey are especially good, so much so that one sometimes regrets the strained intrusions of their aged host and mentor.  Even on the less-successful pairings it's hard to entirely dismiss the notion that Mr Bennett is somewhat creepily leeching on the fresh, nubile talents of his partners in order that he may continue to haunt our nights a little longer.  Which is why I've come to think of these as The Vampire Duets.  He also loses at least one star because so many of these songs simply do not lend themselves to a duet.  What, pray tell, is going on with \\"The Girl I Love\\", the decidedly muddle-headed duet with Sheryl Crow?  It also has to be said that the visual image conjured by Carrie Underwood crooning \\"It Had To Be You\\" to the (great?)grandfatherly Bennett is more than a little uncomfortable.  Ditto Faith Hill marvelling at him in \\"The Way You Look Tonight\\".  Finally, the Taste Police must surely requisition a star for the shameless over-production on most tracks, the phoney showbiz banter that would have us believe that Tony and John/Willie/Josh/et al are BFFs, and the sheer cheesiness of Bennett and Lady Gaga on \\"The Lady Is A Tramp\\".  If Ethel Merman and Mary Martin came back to life and sang a duet on Letterman like they did back in the 60s you'd surely be struck by the incongruity.  For all their greatness, it would seem hokey and embarrassing in the 21st century.  Which is exactly how I feel about Bennett and Gaga romping through \\"The Lady Is A Tramp\\" as though there is some common ground in there somewhere, either lyrically or artistically.  It's a bit like watching your grandfather dirty dance with a wild teenager, who thinks it's hilarious to respond by sticking her tongue in his ear.  You can chuckle and say \\"There's life in the old boy yet\\".  But you know it's wrong.

I thoroughly enjoyed the book TYFS, which I still rate as one of Christopher Buckley's best.  In movie form TYFS is even more impactful, due to the stronger sensory experience of the cinema, while being equally funny and insightful - but not, of course, as pleasingly long or in-depth.  What makes TYFS succeed so brilliantly is Buckley's satirical behind-the-scenes view of how Washington politics and politicians work, and what they say, feel and do, both in public and behind closed doors (which, of course, is quite a contrast).  Although certain scenes in TYFS are a bit over-the-top, and one might quibble with certain things the characters say and do, one gets the sense that there is a lot more truth about our political system in TYFS than there is in today's 6 o'clock evening news.  Movie writer/director Jason Reitman faithfully transfers Buckley's views to the silver screen, and the production values of TYFS are top-notch.  Aaron Eckhart us simply amazing as lead character Nick Naylor: he is perfectly cast as the handsome, well-spoken lead lobbyist for the tobacco industry, and he gives a great performance.  The supporting cast also turns in a series of fine performances: notable are Katie Holmes as an unscrupulous investigative reporter, Maria Bello as a fellow lobbyist (for the alcoholic beverages industry), Robert Duvall as the patriarch of the tobacco industry, and Sam Elliott as the original Marlboro Man.  The settings and cinematography are outstanding, and the producers even got Joan Lunden and Dennis Miller for a couple of talk show scenes.  Any quibbles about the movie aren't worth mentioning, aside from the fact that the movie is definitely for adults, even though there's nothing explicit about the visual images.  So if you are someone who takes a skeptical, perhaps cynical, view of the goings-on in Washington D.C., and would enjoy laughing at how you suspect Washington really works, then you will thoroughly enjoy \\"Thank You For Smoking\\".
