1594202842|4.0|I couldn't help but feel sympathy for Amy Chua after I finished this book. It's rather well written, although disturbing on many levels. It's not that I have major objections about her child-rearing philosophy (I do). It's just that the book shows a highly intelligent, successful woman who is also beset by a profound identity crisis, deep-seeded insecurity and an almost pathological belief of stereotypes, despite her prominent protestations to the contrary.  Chua begins her book with a disclaimer - she is only \\"loosely\\" using the terms \\"Western parent\\" and \\"Chinese parent.\\" She acknowledges that strict parents come from all ethnicities and nationalities. Politically correct platitudes out of the way, for the rest of the book she is right back stereotyping in her binary world: East vs. West; Orient vs. Occident; her parenting philosophy against everybody else's (including her: father, mother, husband, and mother-in-law, it seems). At certain points her daughters and husband point out this cognitive fallacy to her, but it never seems to occur to her that it might be true. She is, after all, a woman hellbent on a mission - to single-handedly stave off the vanguard of impending familial decline represented by her daughters' generation.  What's that, you say? What impending familial decline? Therein lies the fuel that stokes Chua's furnace - a fanatical, possibly pathological trust of stereotypes and trusims. Like a religious fanatic convinced Armageddon is on its way, Chua believes the truism that the grandchildren of immigrants (i.e. her daughters) are destined to lead a slow slide into oblivion. The irony is that the objective likelihood of this decline is all but remote. Among her many accomplishments Chua can boast dual undergraduate/graduate degrees from Harvard; tenure at one of the foremost law schools in the country (Yale); and published author. In addition, she has a sister with a PhD, MD (read: VERY smart doctor) from - where else - Harvard; and is married toanother law faculty member at Yale who was in his younger years an aspiring actor at Juilliard. Hardly the hallmarks of a family pedigree circling the drain.  Chua's actions have the behavioral hallmarks of a person backed into a corner. Placing large bets on music to \\"save\\" her family, her daughters are heavily immersed in the world of classical music performance, with Chua bankrolling extravagant sums on instruments and teachers. Her sacrifices are not just financial, but physical and emotional. Chua scolds, pleads, cajoles and bribes her children into practicing their instruments well into the night while working out the nearly impossible logistics of directly supervising her children's many hours of practice with her career and running a household. These efforts bear fruit when one of her daughters ends up playing in Carnegie Hall.  But that's the problem. Chua's pursuit of objective evidence that she has somehow staved her family's decline is Sisyphean. Her daughters' accomplishments are never enough to allay her worries, and she is quick to interpret even the slightest hint of rebellion (or what she would term \\"non-Chinese behavior\\") from her daughters as a sign of failure. In short, doubt and insecurity pervade her being.  Despite the criticism that Chua's strong-armed parenting style has garnered, it extends from the lack of a cohesive sense of self. Despite outwardly self-identifying as a Asian-American and arriving in the US as an infant, she seems to identify more closely with her Chinese roots. With her inveterate belief in her family's the eventual decline, she fears that her family's Chinese roots will be extinguished with her. This explains her overly aggressive Chinese \\"Tiger\\" parenting method (even shocking her parents) as well as the foisting of an unwanted Chinese identity on her children (who like most American-born children of immigrants likely identify as American).  Luckily for Chua, her daughters are unlikely to end up in psychotherapy. Admittedly, as Chua states children are more resilient than \\"Western parents\\" assume. They are, also, unlikely to end up heralding a decline in her family's fortunes - although this has less to do with her parenting style than who she is. It is more than ironic that Chua felt the \\"Chinese way\\" was the only way to ensure success for her children, while being married to a Jewish man whose upbringing was markedly different culturally/religiously/etc. Despite these differences in upbringing, both grew to become scholars at Yale. This suggests that it's not so much the parenting style, but rather the example that each parent sets and who that parent is. Children of high-flyers will likely become high-flyers themselves, even when left to their own devices - because children emulate those around them. There may be periods of rebellion the road, but these are character-building, if anything. Micromanaged parenting driven by a pathological fear of failure, as in Chua's case, may produce successes but it also runs the risk of producing uncreative, passive automatons.   The devout Amish are comfortable releasing their adolescents into the world to experience all sorts of risk taking and rebellion (rumspringe). Yet a majority of Amish return to the fold; we might all learn from this, take a step back and let our children figure out what is best for them.

0061353248|4.0|Having majored in marketing, minored in economics and had the privilege of teaching both subjects at the college level I'm interested in research that helps to link both fields (i.e. economics and marketing).  Dan Ariley's book contains some insightful research and information that is of practical use to consumers, investors and business people.  I read it shortly after having finished George Akerloff and Robert Schiller's 2009 \\"Animal Spirits,\\" which is an excellent book that discusses the subject of behavioral economics from a more macroeconomic perspective.  I would have given Ariley's book a five rating, except for the research and information in Chapter 5 (The Influence of Arousal) which I found to be morally objectionable and in poor taste with little or no worth as research or practical information -- unless you consider the revelation that college-age males make worse decisions about sex, birth control and STD prevention after watching porn on a computer screen, with a plastic covered key board, to be particularly useful or enlightening \\"research.\\"  Chapter 1, \\"why everything is relative,\\" in addition to introducing the subject of behavioral economics, demonstrates how consumers can be influenced by advertising with product and price display to choose to buy things businesses want us to purchase.  It's closely related to  Chapter 2 on \\"the fallacy of supply and demand\\" which  emphasizes the extent to which demand can be created and manipulated by suppliers, and though it is a good point of emphasis from an economics perspective it is hardly a startling new revelation.  Vance Packard's 1957 Book \\"The Hidden Persuader's\\" may seem a bit dated, but no one since has done a better job in my opinion of describing in detail how marketers use psychology to manipulate and entice consumers to buy their products.  Even so from a consumers perspective it is valuable information to consider when evaluating purchase decisions (i.e. Do I really need or want to buy this, oram I being manipulated to want it by fad, fashion and advertising?)  Chapter 3 on \\"the cost of zero\\" offers a useful insight.  Some of the research though is questionable.  For example the conclusion that accepting a $10 coupon for free versus a $20 coupon for $7 is not optimal is in my view suspect in making unwarranted assumptions.  If I don't want to shop at the store offering the coupon for a discount and don't particularly value their merchandise at more than say 50 cents on the dollar, but will take $10 worth for free, then it's perfectly rational for me to accept the free $10 coupon without offering to pay $7 for a $20 coupon.  Also if I need my cash to buy groceries and gas, and we're talking about a clothing store or electronic outlet coupon, my decision in rejecting the coupon for cash deal is not at all \\"predictably irrational.\\"  Still from a consumer perspective Ariley does a good job of reminding us it's worth considering that a lot of the \\"free\\" stuff businesses offer us isn't worth the time or effort, and may cost us more than it's worth (e.g. especially if we get enticed to pay much more to use the \\"free\\" coupon or to buy a lot of stuff we don't really need).  Chapter 4 on \\"the cost of social norms\\" is really excellent and offers a fresh original view point that would be useful to those studying psychology, marketing, business management or economics.  The idea that there are business and personal circumstances in which paying people to do something may actually motivate them less than allowing them to do it for free is worthy and useful research.  Perhaps one lesson to be applied is that by developing better social relationships both in business and in personal settings we can learn to do a better job of helping others, and in return receive more help and assistance for ourselves in return.  Chapter 6 on \\"the problem of procrastination and self control\\" is a good reminder of the importance of improving our self-discipline and in learning the importance of setting key deadlines and milestones for ourselves and others in reaching our goals.  Another important application is putting a renewed emphasis on disciplining ourselves to save and invest for retirement, and not simply sitting back and hoping social security or a pension will be sufficient.  I somewhat disagree with Ariley (despite his experience to the contrary with student papers at MIT) however in suggesting we might need to put more controls over others to force them to avoid procrastination, even though from a business manger perspective I would say it might suggest the importance of supervisors monitoring deadlines and milestones with subordinates to assure projects stay on schedule.  Self discipline can be learned, and if we incentivize it, and people are held responsible for not meeting their own deadlines, it's a virtue that in my view can be revitalized in modern society.  Chapter 7 on \\"the high price of ownership and why we overvalue what we have\\" might be a good reminder to those who are keeping a lot of extra stuff around to sell it, give it away or trash it to improve their quality of life.  It might also help those looking to sell homes, cars or other items to set realistic prices so they can improve their chances of finding a buyer in a reasonable amount of time.  It might be a good reminder for investors to not hold on to stocks too long once their prospects for price appreciation have dimmed (i.e. don't hesitate to take some profits or cut losses ).  Chapter 8 on \\"keeping doors open\\" emphasizes the importance of making commitments early and also reinforces the lesson of chapter 6 in avoiding procrastination.  We may have lots of options and opportunities in life, but we have limited time and resources to pursue them.  So it's important to choose what is best for us and not to delay too long in making those choices.  Ariley emphasizes there are economic and social costs to such delay, and most of us would do well to heed his advice.  Chapter 9 on the \\"effect of expectations\\" and Chapter 10 on \\"the power of price\\" are very useful and closely related.  Often things such as placebos in medicine work because we expect them to help us, and we often find more enjoyment or relief from a product such as the higher priced pain reliever because we believe paying more gives us more benefit.  By examining those beliefs and doing some research ourselves, we might just be able to save some money by buying lower cost generic drugs, shopping at discount stores and finding lower cost substitutes that work as well or better.  It's worth asking ourselves do we really enjoy it that much more because we paid more for it, and is it really worth the extra price over lower cost alternatives for the benefit it provides?  Chapter 11 and Chapter 12 on \\"the context of our character\\" is also highly useful for understanding ourselves and others regarding our integrity in business and other economic relationships.  For one thing it explains why people are far less likely to steal petty cash from work, but don't mind pilfering office supplies or cheating on expense accounts.  I suggest reading it in conjunction with George Akerloff and Robert Schiller's 2009 Book \\"Animal Spirits\\" in it's discussion of \\"corruption\\" as an undesirable behavior influencing economic decision making, and the importance of keeping it under control in sustaining economic growth and national wealth.  Chapter 13 \\"Beef and Free Lunches\\" ends on a light and humorous note with a discussion of an experiment Ariley conducted at an off campus pub in posing as a waiter to serve drinks (often free) to customers to observe the degree to which their choices were influenced by the choices or suggestions of others.  The one lesson I'll take from it is to try and order first when I'm with a group in a restaurant so I get what I want and am not influenced by the choices of others.

B001U5V03U|3.0|The director, Francesca Zambello, is not working in top form here. She brings to her Royal Opera House DON GIOVANNI more than the rudiments of an interesting visual style, and she certainly shoulders some responsibility for thought about character and theme. But she draws from Simon Keenlyside one of the nastiest, most reptilian and charm-free Dons in my experience, surely her choice more than his, and balances the scales with the platitudes and bromides of a contemporary feminist. Over and over, her blocking emphasizes an idealized sisterhood which crosses class lines, with the three women achieving unity in their victimization and perseverance. A Virgin Mary icon literally watches over this trio of beauties as they bond in struggling against a male-dominated world of abusive brutes (Masetto), unrepentant lechers (Giovanni), and paternalistic patronizers (Ottavio, Commendatore). All three women join hands to wield one weapon against the Don at the close of Act I; Elvira and Zerlina console Anna during \\"Il mio tesoro\\"; \\"Mi tradi\\" is in part an Elvira suicide attempt prevented by the other two. Elvira appears  radiantly happy in the final scene when announcing her plan to spend the rest of her days in the convent. Of  *course* the Elvira of such a production would be happy about that prospect, both for what she is leaving behind and what she is heading toward: spiritually enlightened sisterhood forever.<br /><br />None of this is necessarily invalid, and dumber things have been done with this opera, but it is heavy-handed and anachronistic, and not realized with the subtlety and grace that might have gotten it over. Zambello's interest in the women comes at the expense of the men: the Giovanni and Masetto are just unpleasant, while the more genial Ottavio and Leporello seem undercharacterized, stock. Throughout the opera, parts that tend to play awkwardly even in good productions (the Don's slo-mo flight from Anna near the beginning, with Leporello chuntering away inthe corner) predictably do so here, but easy parts are also muffed (the Don/Commendatore duel; the Don's escape at the first act finale, here done with a rope in the manner of a rock climb; the first Don/Leporello confrontation with the statue, done without a statue). It is a half-baked, neither/nor GIOVANNI, showing the impulse of \\"It's been done so often one way, and I want to do something else\\" without that impulse carried through to a thorough, convincing \\"something else.\\"<br /><br />There are some excellent vocal performances, but that can be said for most DON GIOVANNI DVDs. Most successful are the mezzo Donna Elvira of Joyce DiDonato (a sympathetic and beautifully calibrated acting performance, allied to tight runs and an absolutely seamless voice) and the Leporello of Kyle Ketelsen (vocally wholly agreeable, and well drilled in the standard comedic Leporelloisms to the point of expertise -- but whether his performance has much to do with the production around him, or whether the director's imagination was seized by this character enough to really incorporate him, I am less sure). Also impressive is the Zerlina of Miah Persson, who gets all she can out of Zambello's very sexually liberated and sophisticated take on the peasant girl. The Don Ottavio, Ram&oacute;n Vargas, gives a characteristic performance: never an actor, but sincerely engaged and likable. He sings the part mellifluously, if not as well as he subsequently would in fall 2011 at the Met (another lackluster GIOVANNI, that one by Grandage). In New York, it seemed to me he had achieved the stature of mature artistry; in  London three years earlier, the long-breathed phrases in the middle of \\"Il mio tesoro\\" are a bit labored.<br /><br />If Vargas always seems to be Vargas rather than the character, his basic stage savvy is nevertheless put into sharp relief by the very green Masetto of Robert Gleadow. The latter has a nice voice, but his scenes are hampered by stiffness and diffidence, accompanied by furtive sidelong glances, surprising to encounter in a broadcast performance. No such concerns are raised by Marina Poplavskaya, who throws herself passionately into the production's very black-and-white, unambiguously virtuous and wronged Donna Anna (I did like the detail of the character's mourning veil). Poplavskaya's haunting timbre and compelling presence compensate for the shortish breath line and hit-or-miss fioritura. Zambello shrewdly has the Russian soprano let down her waist-length blond tresses as she prepares to navigate the rapids of the second half of \\"Non mi dir\\" -- the distraction is welcome. Eric Halfvarson is only a serviceable Commendatore. In the title role, Simon Keenlyside is in rough vocal estate, and portrays a facet of the character (brutality) with zeal and professionalism, while other possibilities are deliberately underexposed.<br /><br />I find the contribution of the conductor, the late Sir Charles Mackerras, a challenge to review. Speeds are on the fast side, attacks are aggressive, phrasing pushy; the period-style horns rasp away authentically, the timpanist clubs away with his hard sticks, and the overall sonority elicited is thin and watery. It is surely the performance Mackerras was trying to conjure up, it is academically defensible, and I am sure it is the way someone else likes music of this vintage to go. I found it a trial of the nerves, but my preference for the richer and sleeker sounds of, say, Riccardo Muti (especially on his second DVD, the one from Vienna), is a subjective one. What is more clearly problematic is the poor sound balance, every time to the detriment of the vocal soloists. I wondered how this came off in the house, and whether the octogenarian conductor was suffering a not-atypical decline in hearing acuity.<br /><br />Extra features are very good: a comprehensive behind-the-scenes video tour of the ROH hosted by Deborah Bull (not directly linked to this DON GIOVANNI; the Hytner DON CARLO was being prepared at the time), and five minutes each allotted for music director Antonio Pappano (whom I wish had conducted) to interrogate Mackerras and Zambello. Pappano's questions are better than the inane ones the Met's star singers typically pose to each other on Saturday afternoon movie broadcasts (e.g. \\"Is it difficult to go from tragedy to comedy?\\"), and Zambello is so thoughtful and poised that I wish I had enjoyed the product of her efforts more this time.

B000PMLFRA|2.0|I loved the original movies, but my imagination, and modern technology, conspired against me so much so that when I first heard that George was updating the originals with more added scenes, I thought, \\"Cool!  Gotta see that!\\".  I'm not a purist.  If adding something to something great makes something that is great even greater, I say...uh...great.  Anyway, I actually love all the CGI changes except two, and one of them I could live with...  The change I can live with: Han no longer shoots first-  I didn't really notice the first (and only) time I saw Star Wars: A New Hope in the theater that Han shot first, nor did I realize how important to his character development that scene was.  It was the audience's first encounter with this anti-hero who would soon become one of the most beloved heroes in movie history.  However, a lot of that development in who he was to become was firmly based on who he was to begin with.  So having Han shoot first was really an awesome way to tell the audience what a bad guy he originally was.  Sure, he shot another bad guy, and the other bad guy would have shot Han if Han hadn't shot first, but a bad guy who takes out another bad guy can still be a bad guy.  Right?  But to make the other bad guy just a bad shot, and to give the anti-hero enough of a heart to wait until his protagonist attempted to kill him before he took his shot was a pretty major, and disappointing, decision by Mr. Lucas.  But I could have lived with that.  The scene was short, sweet, and not enough to ruin the trilogy   (note: you may have noticed I didn't include the prequel trilogy as part of the series; I still hold out hope that someday in my lifetime a new and upcoming Star Wars fan and director with the same focus Lucas had in 1977 will re-make the prequels, and Jar-Jar with only be a humorous footnote in the margin of Star Wars history.  I hold out the same kind of hope for the Harry Potter movies, but that's another review, and a lot less traumatic).  The change I CANNOT live with: Little Annoying Annie replacing Papa Darth-  However, having said all that, the scene that ruined EVERYTHING Star Wars for me (except the Dark Knight and Legos video games) was digitally replacing Anakin \\"Big Bad Daddy\\" Skywalker, the man who used to be Darth Vader, one of the most infamous villains-turned-repentant-father in Hollywood lore, who's former visage could chill your bones just by turning his helmet towards the camera, with Anakin \\"Annie\\" Skywalker, the teenage whiner who was only mildly interesting to watch when he got really, really mad.  And even then he flailed his light saber around like a Kung-Fu wannabe while battling his better, Obi-Wan.  Meesa no unnastan da logik beehine dat disizhun!  Seriously, when I started watching the updated CGI'd \\"original\\" trilogy on DVD, I was digging the experience (\\"re-digging\\"?), and was so excited when at the end of Empire, I saw that Mr. Lucas had chosen to make the victory celebration more appropriately celebratory.  One of the few issues I had with the original was the wimpy musical number selected to end this epic experience. Imagine my horror (too strong a word?  I think not!), when, while emotionally engaged in the final scenes, our heroes and innocents happy, reunited, entire planets celebrating in a fashion appropriate to the occasion and scope, with space-high fireworks and various species-appropriate orchestral music, not just a bunch of paper mache koalas singing \\"Kum Bi Ya\\" in aboriginese, and I'm thinking, \\"Yeah!  Awesome!\\", the scene that carries the most weight and biggest pay-off arrives.  Obi-Wan, Yoda and Anakin Skywalker (Luke's FATHER.  Not his brother, not his cousin, not his roommate) appear as we last remember them: healthy but aged and wise.  But wait.  What?  Who's that kid where Daddy Darth should be standing, uh, floating?  It isn't Anakin Skywalker as we remember him, but as we've been trying to forget him: as the whiny, non-heroic, non-repentant, goofily-grinningTEENAGER, healthy yes, but aged?  Nuh-uh.  Wise?  Not even.  Not to put to fine a point on it, but not even in the right trilogy!!!  Put back the old guy Mr. Lucas should.  Since that terrible experience (I still mumble about it in my car on the way to and from work), I haven't even had the desire to go back to my DVDs, let alone buy them on Blu-Ray!.  (deep breath) Anyway, I think we all get where I'm coming from.  I digress.  Sorry, Mr, Lucas, but if you were to ever bump into me on the street, and then, out of the blue, were to ask me (and why wouldn't you?) what I thought you could do to get me to watch anything Star Wars EVER AGAIN, I would tell you, \\"Mr. Lucas...\\" (no reason to be disrespectful), I would say, \\"Mr. Lucas, in my humble opinion, your position in the sci-fi universe would be elevated from the current Emperor of the Imperial Army, back to Original Esteemed Jedi Master, by first of all returning Sebastian Shaw...\\" (the man who played Vader unmasked; don't be too impressed, \\"R. White\\" set me straight on this) \\"...to his proper place as the spirit of St. Anakin, and second by giving Han Solo back his anti-heroic dignity, and letting him shoot first.  That, good sir, would not only make me want to continue viewing your original trilogy in CGI, but I would then HAVE to buy the Blu-Ray version, because then, and only then, would STAR WARS IV, V & VI return to the top of my ten-best-movies-of-all-time list (at, coincidentally, positions IV, V and VI, only behind the Lord of the Rings trilogy)!!  Plus I would quit complaining to anyone who will listen about Anakin the Ghost-Child, and start gratefully recommending the original trilogy, on BluRay, to all my friends!!\\"  And no, just because Star Wars beats Star Trek, that does not make me a Star Wars fanatic.  There are other reasons...  So there you have it.  Just a casual fan, briefly (!) expressing his opinions about a movie series that was created 30 years ago.  No big deal.  Thanks for reading.  Fight for the Light!  Duke Snyder  Anaheim, CA, USA

0321704452|3.0|When I first skimmed the syllabus for EGR 641 and saw the book title The Naked Presenter, I kind of chuckled at the thought of being naked while presenting. My first thought was that this was going to be a book discussing the humor behind presentations - perhaps a collection of stories making light of the oppressive and dreaded presentation utilizing slideware. I purchased the book and began to read; I was initially disappointed - I was looking forward to this humorous book I'd psyched myself up for. Anyway, I sat down on a Saturday and read the book cover to cover. Surprise - I came away feeling no longer disappointed, but more relieved that I now have a fantastic tool-book that perfectly complements and reinforces what I'm already learning in my Professional Communications class. While I really enjoyed Garr Reynolds' zen-like approach to delivering presentations, I also enjoyed the more cosmetic aspects of the book - the print quality, the layout, and the ease of the read. But, generally I left the read with one aspect of The Naked Presenter that resonated most with me: Allow your presence to manifest by engaging the audience in passion, proximity, and play.   I learned that you must properly engage your audience to become a great presenter. \\"When it comes to presentation delivery, the problem generally is not the display of too much emotion but rather the utter lack of it\\" (Reynolds 100).  According to Reynolds, the power of the smile - the sincere smile is infectious and contagious. This I also agreed with and have found to be true. Ensuring that you tap into your audience's emotions; utilize sincerity in smiling; interact in proximity; and encourage play via tone and facial expressions - these are all valuable lessons that Reynolds thankfully expands on.  The only criticism I have of the book is that there was no true exploration on the tricks and trades of slideware - or Reynolds' dissection of slideware tools he found to be most effective and why. Also he didn't share his tips or tricks of the trade with physical or technical trip-ups. (I think that the only trick he shares utilizes the B key for a black screen.). He does discuss the process of developing your presentations by brainstorming, sketching visuals, etc., but I would have liked to learn more about the products out there that get his stamp of approval for presenting.   However, The Naked Presenter, is a book that teaches you how to project yourself. After all - that's what a presentation is (a projection of you). Reading Reynolds' work teaches you that the power behind the presentation is not in your slides, but in the presenter. The presenter is the key to what message the audience comes away with. I highly recommend The Naked Presenter and am pleased with its principles. I plan to practice these principles right away beginning first with showing my jounetsu, passion.

It's like he never left and she feels attracted to him for who he is.<br /><br />But of course there are obstacles in the way. The boyfriend Max, Crickett being away at college, her family, his family, of course there has to be problems separating them. I'm going to stop right there, I don't want to ruin one bit of the plot. I want you to experience the magic of Lola And The Boy Next Door all by yourself.<br /><br />And yes, there are some Etienne and Anna throughout the book. They are in it quite a bit actually which was kind of exciting.<br /><br />When I started Lola And The Boy Next Door, I knew I was going to enjoy it. There is just something magical about Perkin's writing style. She makes you feel alive. She makes you feel like you are really the girl who is in love with the boy. She makes your heart jump, she makes your heart ache. It was charming, cute, sweet, brain candy, and realistic.<br /><br />I loved Crickett. I loved how he made a bridge to connect their windows, I loved how he wore his pants tight, I loved the bracelets he wore and how he had to write everything down. I loved his love for his family. I loved how sweet he was.<br /><br />I loved Lola too. I loved her sense of style, I loved how you could tell she wanted to be strong, but sometimes it just got to her and she had to let it out. I loved how she would speak her mind most of the time, I loved the different wigs and clothes she wore. I loved how she was proud of it.<br /><br />Point blank, I loved Lola and the Boy Next Door. And this is the exact kind of book that makes me love YA. I adore Perkins. I adore Crickett and Etienne. How Perkins can create two totally different guys and make me fall in love with each of them the same is incredible.<br /><br />Lola And The Boy Next Door was everything I hoped it would be. I did like Anna And The French Kiss a tad more but I think that has to do with Anna going to Paris, and who wouldn't want to meet a hot french guy?<br /><br />Lola focuses more on having the boy you are meant to be with right in front of your eyes all your life. It has to do with romance, soul-mates, magic.<br /><br />The whole time I was reading Lola I was mesmerized. There is just this sweetness about it that makes me want to jump up and down, and smile, and laugh. And be happy.<br /><br />Overall: If you couldn't tell by now, I loved Lola! I want to go on a date with this book. A little extreme huh? Well, can I take Crickett on a date instead? Just for one night? Please Please Lola? If you haven't picked up Lola And The Boy Next Door, do it now! Go order it right now! B0025UHKNS|1.0|Here are my impressions after having the watch for about 3 days and using it a few times in that period.  I used it for an ~7-8 mile run, 8k on a rowing machine, another walk interacting with the basic features. My background: I'm a fairly serious and toward-the-front but absolutely-not-placing runner (5ks to ultras, roads and trails):    1) HRM did not \\"just work\\" out of the box.  After lots of frustrating attempts to get it paired, including jiggling the battery and trying a new battery, I *again* tried to re-insert the CR2032 and finally must have not had a good connection the first ~dozen times because during \\"Searching...\\" from the HRM setup, it immediately said that a HRM was detected.  I can't speak to the accuracy yet, but pairing was much flakier than I thought it should have been.    2) I really dislike the location of the \\"lap\\" button.  For runners (and IMO) this is the most important button and it should be the easiest one to find and press for workouts.  Setting up \\"auto-lap\\" functions with the GPS is definitely NOT better - you want to start and split on points on a track, not a dependency on when the GPS thinks you've crossed some threshold.  The lap button is one of the physical buttons on the lower right.  My old Timex had a large, flat button on under the face of the display which was super easy to find and press.  I'm not trying to nitpick a small bit of the design - I really think this is important on a watch like this which is why I call it out.    3) Not crazy about the displays during training.  For some reason, it keeps throwing up a big banner \\"Hold reset to save activity\\" obscuring most of the display. I'm sure I'm doing something wrong and I'm sure I don't care right now about saving activity, I just want a stopwatch to go and tell me some splits.  Also, I don't like the layout of the time values in the UI - it displays the following (laid out like this)    AGGREGATE_TIME <-- this is big  LAP_PACE TOTAL_DISTANCE <-- these are smaller    Again, for a workout I would much rather see my split time, but the watch doesn't give me an option for this (after you mash the \\"Lap\\" button, you see a display for ~10 seconds showing that split time).  I can review this extensively afterward, but I can't see it during the workout (at least not easily - maybe I just haven't figured it out yet).  I haven't trained with a GPS in a while to have an opinion on whether I would rather see lap distance vs. total distance, but as far as I can tell, the device doesn't give me the option so hopefully I want total distance.    4) general design and overall feel - these are good.  The HRM is like others I've used but other reviewers give it high marks so I'm guessing it will be accurate (I can say it feels identical to others I've used before). The GPS locks fast and seems OK, which is my basic expectation for a device like this in 2011. The watch feels a lot less bulky than I expected (though I would still only ever wear it during exercise), which is a significant plus. I very much dislike the bezel. This is pretty, but aggravatingly not easy to use / takes time to learn.  To interact with it, you tap, tap and hold, or slide to scroll between menus.  I believe buttons would be more precise and more intuitive, but uglier and much less sleek. It's obvious from the reviews that others either prioritize differently or that I'm just clumsy or haven't learned to interact with this very well yet, but that's my initial feeling on this.    5) a couple more things I'm not crazy about: I used to have a 10* series Forerunner.  The GPS was slow to lock and this was like having a graphing calculator strapped to your wrist, but I really liked the small map UI that this device offered.  I once took it on a backcountry snowboarding trip with friends and that device helped me realize that on our descent we had veered far from our uphill course. It might have saved out lives.  The 405 really can't do this because it seems that its \\"return to start\\" behavior only gives you a compass reading for your trajectory to get back to the srart (the 10* had a small map). The manual indicates tha the battery will only last about 8 hours. This would not have been long enough to get data for a 50 mile trail race I did last summer (I guess I should get faster!)    6) the offered \\"workouts\\" functions look pretty good, but I haven't tried using these much yet.    Overall, my initial sense is that I like a lot of the data this looks like it will capture, but it seems better for people who want to import the data into a computer so they can look back on it after the workout than if you want it to help you measure and improve performance DURING a workout. It also seems to miss what I believe to be basic important design elements for such a device (how you get and interact with lap splits), however it does LOOK good.  A lot of this is influenced by having a 10 year old Timex that stores 50 lap splits (more than you can usually find) that is simple and works really well, so I'll try to come back and revisit this review when I've spent more time with the device. So for now, even though most of my feedback is critical, I'm evaluating at 3 stars because I want to give it the benefit of the doubt that it will impress me over time with its more powerful features.    UPDATE: I've completed my first and probably only workout since getting the HRM to pair and it's inaccurate enough that I have to demote this product to 1 star.  The watch seems kinda OK (more on that) but the HRM is just laughably inaccurate. The workout I did was three repeats of an ~5.5 mile loop on trails that includes some elevation change.  Here are the stats from the watch: [...]    Before starting, I wet the HRM and it was in the correct place on my chest and I'd say it was on quite snugly (i.e. I believe I was using it right).  If you look at the HRM data, it says I spent most of the workout at 230bpm+ and finished with an average 199bpm. I'm pretty sure - and I'm going to check this again now - nope, my heart did *not* explode today, so I think this is probably wrong. After the workout I hit the split to cool down for about 5 minutes and looked at the HRM again and after those 5 minutes it said I was at 234bpm!     So, the heart rate monitor is, in my opinion, terrible.  Let's go back to just the watch for a minute:    The frustration I mentioned in my original review about the split button is still affirmed.  I had trouble hitting the split and when it doesn't work, I naturally compensate by holding the watch with another finger opposite the split button but this results in an interaction with the touch bezel and my split not getting recorded. This might be the type of thing I'd adapt to with the watch, but it's annoying to need to adapt instead of this just working.    I'm pretty positive now that there isn't any way to customize what data is shown on the training screen and I think it's not designed well for people who are serious about running. Cumulative time is less important than split time, Garmin - this isn't news.    I established that the HRM definitely works poorly for me but the HRM data + watch calculation of my caloric burn earns the watch itself another ding, too.  I'm 6' and weigh >180 pounds and the Garmin has this profile data about me.  Somehow, the watch estimated this run (16.25 miles with 3400' net elevation change) to be a 1359 calorie run.  First, I don't believe that - this is a challenging run and I was moving at about an 8:48 pace (the total pace calculated on the Garmin connect page is off because there's a 5 minute split at the end where I was standing around the parking lot to collect HRM data but did not move).  That would put my net (I assume this is showing net - if it's gross it's even worse) caloric burn rate at 83.5 calories/mile (keep in mind, the watch thought this was a 199bpm workout). I don't even know how it could project that.  I'd love to think that this accurately reflects my efficiency as a runner, but I think it's just a lot more likely that this product deserves a 1 star review, so here it is.

0634022296|5.0|I recently bought this songbook with the hopes that it would provide more chord-based instruction rather than tablature and standard notation. Right away, I knew I hit a home run. The layouts of every song penned by the four Beatles is very easy to follow, with each chord chart displayed. The key changes are mapped over the lyrics, so you can sing along and make the changes as you read through. The chords and keys are completely accurate. The only negative I can think of regarding this book is the absence of time signatures and tempo suggestions at the start of each song. But this is only a minor inconvenience. It is best anyway to listen to the songs first and remember the feel and tempo of the guitars (or other rhythm instrument). The book works well for learning in conjunction with the recordings. Also of note is that this book does not include cover songs performed by The Beatles. On some of their early records, The Beatles recorded their own versions of songs by mostly American stars like Chuck Berry, Buddy Holly, and many different R&B groups, so if you are interested in learning The Beatles' versions of these songs, you'll have to find them elsewhere. Included in the book are some very early Lennon/McCartney/Harrison songs which never made entry into The Beatles official recorded canon. I highly recommend this book, as it is educational and very fun to flip through, whether you're a kid learning to play guitar, or a studio professional.

B003UESJF6|4.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />  Despite its rather conventional ending, 'Megamind' proves to be a highly entertaining twist on the superhero genre. Satirizing the origin of the Superman comics stories, we're introduced to two different superheros, Megamind and Metroman, as they arrive on earth from separate dying planets, in space capsules at the same time. Metroman's  is smooth but Megamind's is anything but--crashing into various meteors and debris like an out of control bumper car. A flashback chronicles how Metroman ends up with an all-American family but Megamind plops down in middle of the Metro City prison where he's raised by inmates to be a 'villain'. But Megamind is actually more like The Wizard of Oz than a hardened criminal--his dark side can best be described as a mischievous youthful offender, capable of rehabilitation.<br /><br />  If Megamind's delinquent mindset is his Achilles heel, Metroman is also trapped by his celebrity status. An Adonis with a chiseled jaw, Metroman flings babies in the air with aplomb as police officer shoot their guns as part of a blind celebratory approbation. Megamind and Metroman periodically battle one another for the affection of love object Roxanne (Roxie) Ritchie, a perky TV newscaster voiced by the talented Tina Fey. Roxie isn't impressed by their clumsy attempts to win her affections. She's bored after being kidnapped by Megamind and after being brought to his lair bluntly tells Megamind that your \\"plan never works\\". When Metroman arrives and engages Megamind in yet another physical confrontation that always ends in a stalemate, Roxie wittily queries: \\"Girls, girls, you're both pretty. Can I go home now?\\"<br /><br />  'Megamind' breaks into its second act when Metroman fakes his own death and the blue-skinned Conehead finds himself experiencing an 'existential crisis'. Without Metroman, Megamind laments, \\"it's just too easy now\\". In his 'despair', Megamind's delinquency in Metro City (or 'Metrocity' as he puts it) multiplies exponentially. Megamind steals all the artwork in the city's museums and places them inside his own office inside City Hall. He throws currency all over the streets as if it's confetti, but none of the mischief makes him happy. Finally, using an idea he appropriates from Roxie, he decides to create his own superhero so he can once again find some meaning in his existence. NY Times critic Stephen Holden puts it most succinctly: \\"The movie's central joke -- that good and evil are meaningless unless both exist in continual opposition -- is unusually sophisticated for an animated movie.\\"<br /><br />  Megamind's new opponent turns out to be Roxie's cameraman, Hal, a geek who expects women to flock to him without mustering any effort to charm them. Before being injected with Megamind's new preparation that alters his DNA, Hal is a harmless nerd who reminds me of a caricature of Bill Murray's cameraman in the classic comedy, 'Groundhog Day'.<br /><br />  There are tons of funny moments in 'Megamind'. Here a few that come to mind: when Megamind tells Roxie they're making renovations at the Metroman Museum (\\"we're having the walls and ceilings removed\\"); when Megamind throws the dynamite at his mini-bots and apologizes (\\"Daddy's sorry\\"); Minion's expression when he leaves Megamind (stifling his tears and then displaying a stiff upper lip); Titan's lame comment after Roxie rejects him (\\"Wow, our first fight\\"); the reaction of the man with the baby in the restaurant when Bernard is transformed back to Megamind (his eyes are wide open in disbelief!).<br /><br />  Despite all the good stuff, 'Megamind' loses its way when introducing 'Titan', a character that diminishes the comic tone of the film's second half. Although Megamind was always up to mischief , he never had murderous intentions (remember how surprised he was, when he thought he had killed Metroman). Before Megamind's misguided intervention, Hal was like many other geeky guys who simplyhadn't discovered the right way to charm women. Remember it was Roxanne herself who confronted Titan and reminded him that he was not such a bad guy after all, BEFORE he was transformed. This is what Roxanne says to Titan right before he ties her up on the tower: \\"I want to talk to the real Hal...I want to talk to the guy who liked being a cameraman, and eating dip and being a nerd and being not as scary as the Titan Hal.\\" In Roxanne's view, before the injection, Hal may have been \\"self-absorbed\\" and perhaps a little \\"selfish\\", but certainly not in her eyes, a creepy stalker. He had his \\"issues\\" just like Megamind and Metroman, but they were all multi-dimensional characters.<br /><br />  Only Titan is a one-note character. He has no charm and doesn't really fit in the Megamind universe due to his unpleasant attitude and non-stop nastiness. When he actually tries to kill Roxanne by lobbing the bus at her, the movie suddenly turns in a rather sour, unfunny direction. I realize that once he was injected, all of Hal's bad points were magnified to the point where he became a psychopath. But why was that? Why couldn't Hal remain the goofy nerd, even after Megamind injected him? Everything else in the movie was FUN, but once Titan turned into who he was, things weren't much fun anymore.<br /><br />  In essence, there needed to be some kind of explanation in the script as to WHY Titan became a psychopath. Perhaps he encountered some Kryptonite type substance that made him act that way or perhaps Minion used the wrong DNA when the formula was being prepared. Some may believe on the other hand that Hal was innately bad and Titan merely represented a magnification of his evil nature. Whatever the case, the Titan character was so unlikeable that the film's denouement turned into the standard comic book 'good triumphs over evil' narrative.<br /><br />  Why belabor Megamind's obvious shortcomings? There's enough excellent, clever stuff here to keep the critical film goer eminently satisfied. 'Megamind' is without a doubt, worth more than just one viewing. Check it out!was Roxanne herself who confronted Titan and reminded him that he was not such a bad guy after all, BEFORE he was transformed. This is what Roxanne says to Titan right before he ties her up on the tower: \\"I want to talk to the real Hal...I want to talk to the guy who liked being a cameraman, and eating dip and being a nerd and being not as scary as the Titan Hal.\\" In Roxanne's view, before the injection, Hal may have been \\"self-absorbed\\" and perhaps a little \\"selfish\\", but certainly not in her eyes, a creepy stalker. He had his \\"issues\\" just like Megamind and Metroman, but they were all multi-dimensional characters.    Only Titan is a one-note character. He has no charm and doesn't really fit in the Megamind universe due to his unpleasant attitude and non-stop nastiness. When he actually tries to kill Roxanne by lobbing the bus at her, the movie suddenly turns in a rather sour, unfunny direction. I realize that once he was injected, all of Hal's bad points were magnified to the point where he became a psychopath. But why was that? Why couldn't Hal remain the goofy nerd, even after Megamind injected him? Everything else in the movie was FUN, but once Titan turned into who he was, things weren't much fun anymore.    In essence, there needed to be some kind of explanation in the script as to WHY Titan became a psychopath. Perhaps he encountered some Kryptonite type substance that made him act that way or perhaps Minion used the wrong DNA when the formula was being prepared. Some may believe on the other hand that Hal was innately bad and Titan merely represented a magnification of his evil nature. Whatever the case, the Titan character was so unlikeable that the film's denouement turned into the standard comic book 'good triumphs over evil' narrative.    Why belabor Megamind's obvious shortcomings? There's enough excellent, clever stuff here to keep the critical film goer eminently satisfied. 'Megamind' is without a doubt, worth more than just one viewing. Check it out!

B005UT29Z0|3.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />  'Margin Call' is first time writer/director J.C. Chandor's distillation of the 2008 financial meltdown. Chandor creates a compilation firm based on Lehman Brothers and other Wall Street firms that tanked during the Wall Street economic crisis. The action is compressed down to one evening and a following morning despite the fact that the actual meltdown took place over a few months. The characters are entirely fictional and I heard that Chandor, during a talk at the new Lincoln Center film center, created the characters without consulting anyone specifically on Wall Street but then had some friends who were in the financial industry, look the script over once it had been completed.<br /><br />  The plot is rather simple. Things come to head at Chandor's fictional firm, when seasoned risk management executive Eric Dale (Stanley Tucci) is fired after another company downsizing. As he's being escorted from the building, he hands his subordinate, former physics ace, Peter Sullivan (Zachary Quinto), a flash drive containing a snapshot of the seemingly deteriorating financial situation at the firm (one wonders why 'security' allowed him to hand the flash drive over to Sullivan). A series of executives then are informed of the crisis, culminating in the arrival of the big boss, John Tuld (Jeremy Irons) who decides to sell off most of the company's assets resulting in big profits for the few executives and employees left at the firm but curtains for the rest of the underlings as well as a nasty kick in the pants to the overall US economy.<br /><br />  Frankly, Chandor's attempt to dramatize the economic crisis is admirable but the subject matter doesn't really lend itself to palpable drama. Part of the problem is that many people (including myself) don't really understand how the financial system works. Chandor goes to great lengths to provide a simplified explanation for the financial collapse, but to be honest I still don't understand what it's all about, despite all of Chandor's attempts at transparency.<br /><br />  Without a tension-filled plot, Chandor must fall back on strong characterizations of the principal players. Jeremy Irons really saves the day as CEO Tuld. Irons is the only actor amongst the solid cast, to turn his amoral financial executive into a flesh and blood human being. He also happens to have the best lines of dialogue in the script. Irons serves up a superb speech at the film's climax, detailing his cynical philosophy that financial downturns always occur in history and are unavoidable.<br /><br />  The rest of the cast do their best in roles that are sorely underdeveloped. Quinto neatly conveys the integrity of a former \\"rocket scientist\\" who admits taking a job on Wall Street, simply \\"for the money\\". But Kevin Spacey struggles in his role as Sam, the ethical company man who refuses to quit because he also \\"needs the money\\". Chandor awkwardly attempts to humanize Spacey's Sam in the final scene, where the executive attempts to bury his recently deceased dog, under his ex-wife's lawn. The problem is that we don't find out enough about Sam to care about him. The same goes Paul Bettany as Will, whose main claim to fame is that he hangs out at strip clubs. Simon Baker does his best as the cold as steel executive Jared but he too lacks a back story. Ditto for Demi Moore's Sarah Robertson, who's thrown under the bus by CEO Fuld right before the big sell-off. Stanley Tucci ends up with the sorry sad sack role as the canned supervisor, who also throws principles to the wind by returning to the company one last time, after being threatened with retaliation by the sinking firm.<br /><br />  Mr. Chandor must be commended for not only bringing the story of the the great 2008 financial collapse to the small screen but ensuring there were no happy endings. As in real life, the traders and executives broke no rules in this unregulated industry and hence were only guilty of ethical lapses, which of course were not punishable in a criminal court of law.<br /><br />  There are those who may be satisfied with Chandor's sketchy and generic take on the financial collapse that precipitated the current financial downturn, but there are many more books and documentaries that will provide a more thoughtful and detailed reckoning of what really went on Wall Street during those heady times. If however, you wish to start with the basics, 'Margin Call' should be your baby.f ethical lapses, which of course were not punishable in a criminal court of law.<br /><br />  There are those who may be satisfied with Chandor's sketchy and generic take on the financial collapse that precipitated the current financial downturn, but there are many more books and documentaries that will provide a more thoughtful and detailed reckoning of what really went on Wall Street during those heady times. If however, you wish to start with the basics, 'Margin Call' should be your baby.

1451627289|5.0|With such a vast catalog of work, it has become increasingly difficult for Stephen King's output of the last ten years or so to measure up to the past, or to be deemed \\"King-worthy,\\" so to speak.  Mixed opinions abound when it comes to the final three Dark Tower books, Cell, Lisey's Story, Duma Key, and Under the Dome, not to mention  the Bachman novel Blaze and the two collections, Just After Sunset and Full Dark, No Stars.  Despite this long-running lack of concensus, 11/22/63 looks like a bit of a comeback for the Stephen King brand.  For one, noted director Jonathan Demme signed on before the book had even been released to helm the film in one of the fastest turn-arounds from a book's release to a movie adaptation King has seen in years.  When an Academy Award-winning director wants your manuscript before the public even sees it, it's usually an indication of a pretty good book.  Add to that the fact that it's being praised by critics and fans both and 11/22/63 comes out a success all around.<br /><br />As a longtime King fan, which admittedly adds a certain bias, I was fascinated by the story, which went in different directions than I expected.  When I first heard that King was writing a book about going back in time to save JFK, I was interested, but less than thrilled.  Time travel is hardly an original concept, nor is the idea of changing the past, so I wasn't sure where King could go with that premise.  Well, he answered my doubts and then some, as far as I'm concerned.  The simplicity of the rabbit hole is perfect, and classic Stephen King.  I'm glad he didn't try to go the sci-fi route with some sort of time machine; instead, he chose a sort of interdimensional portal, not too different from those used in the Dark Tower books.  I was also captivated by the concept of the past fighting back, creating obstacles in proportion to the changes attempting to be made. It all had a feasibility that allowed me to completely suspend my disbelief, as the inexplicable existence of the hole, its random location, and its odd \\"rules\\" (opening on the same date and resetting with each use) made it seem that much more real, and made for incredibly fun storytelling.<br /><br />The book did satisfy my expectations of actually following Lee Harvey Oswald around and intimately observing his furtive lifestyle.  It felt genuinely chilling to experience him as a literary character, and looking over his shoulder had a dark, voyeuristic thrill that kept me turning pages long into the night.  As far as the assassination itself, King ignores some of the more plausible conspiracy theories, but his narrative tells the story of Jake Epping and Sadie, not of Oswald, so that makes sense.  I was almost disappointed when I first realized the book would go this route, but the characters and their stories are so rich and compelling that I lost that feeling quickly.  Jake, in particular, is one of King's best protagonists in many years, a thoroughly likeable, realistic character whose motivations are noble but believable.  He's very easy to root for, and you agonize over his pain.  Sadie and Deke are great characters, too.  King did a fine job of delivering both a mesmerizing plot and fully drawn characters.<br /><br />Speaking of delivering, the end of this novel does exactly that.  Given some of the ambiguity in recent King novels, I thought much would go unexplained, with Kennedy maybe being shot by someone other than Oswald without revealing the shooter, or Kennedy being saved but the changes to the future not being shown.  Not so.  Like early King novels, most everything is explained in an amazing conclusion that idealizes nothing.  King might be a liberal politically, but he has no agenda here to that end, knowing like previous authors of time-travel fiction that altering the past could never lead to a positive outcome, and certainly not to anything we could predict with any accuracy.  The infinite variables make the idea impossible, and readers andJake learn this together in heartbreaking fashion.  While I do have a preference for King as one of his fans, you don't have to take my word for it that 11/22/63 is a great book. Matt Damon meets a beautiful woman that he falls in love with (of course, just *look* at her!) and every time he loses contact with the woman, he meets her again by total chance either during a bus ride or while walking down the city sidewalk, which means this is NOT part of the plan! Basically the moral is that you have choices in your life, and free will, so you should take advantage of it.

B0054TVW04|4.0|In \\"Civilization: The West and the Rest\\", Niall Ferguson presents a lively, grand analysis of why Western Culture has dominated the world for the last 500 years.  In this he follows in the footsteps of Jared Diamond's \\"Guns, Germs, and Steel\\" as well as David Landes's \\"The Poverty and Wealth of Nations\\" (n.b., the best of the lot).  The book is entertaining and thought provoking.  It is also timely in that it covers the recent economic crash and also predicts the outcome of our future relations with China.   Ferguson suffers by comparison, however, to Diamond and Landes.  In particular, his presentation is not as cogent.  Sometimes he seems to make his points by delineating their negative space - we are given a lengthy list of what is not the case, but then left to our own devices to sort out what is left.  Another problem is the uneven prose.  The most unfortunate, wince inducing expression Ferguson uses is his characterization of Western Civilization's primary strengths (viz.,  decentralization of political and economic life, science, property rights, medicine, the consumer society, work ethic) as its \\"killer apps\\".  On the other hand, I enjoyed his joking reference to \\"Resterners\\", the opposite of \\"Westerners\\".  Ferguson does not shy away from controversy.  For example, he suggests that imperialism provided some benefits to the conquered peoples, and also updates Max Weber in suggesting that Protestantism was an essential ingredient to the commercial success of the West.  But he is reasonable rather than dogmatic, thought-provoking rather than bigoted.  \\"Not as good as Landes\\" can still be quite good.  \\"Civilization\\" is a good read, and will give you much to mull over.

1451648537|4.0|I had to read this book as my interest in Jobs and how he thinks has grown over the years.  I had always had the perception that Jobs had genius in finding simplicity where others saw complexity, and the book provides some of examples of this.    The author includes a number of Jobs' quotations, giving the reader another way to gauge how Jobs thought.  I have been surprised to hear some argue that he was no more brilliant many others in the field, in part because he did not write computer code.  I was surprised to see that Gates diminished Jobs on this point, and as a software developer, I believe that design is more important than writing actual code.  The author seemed quite detached from his subject, and I am not sure why.  Perhaps it was his way of maintaining his journalistic objectivity, or perhaps he did not personally like his subject.  The author seemed to favor people like Wozniak and Ives who were personally kind people, whereas Jobs could be cruel, manipulative, and so on.  The author leaves no doubt that Jobs was often quite mean, and one of the author's themes questions why he had to be so.  I don't think he finds the answer.  A problem with the author's detachment, in my opinion, is that he misses the emotion associated with Jobs' presentations.  The iPhone launch, as just one of many possible examples, oozes emotion and shows the relationship Jobs had with his followers.  One can watch that product launch on YouTube and wonder why the coverage in the book lacks any emotion at all.  In that sense, it lacks some accuracy and certainly disappoints.  One does not have to be a fan of Jobs to want to understand the emotion he engendered.  It is impossible to lose the emotion of his tragic fight with cancer which is covered in painful detail.  It is hard to read those sections without wishing the outcome could have been different.    I give the author credit for making the \\"reality distortion field\\" a recurring theme as it does give insight into the man.  Other than this and the cruelty theme, however, the books seems to lack cohesion, feeling instead like a series of unconnected accounts.    Some periods are barely covered, such as his time at NeXT.  Some debated questions go unanswered:  how much of the existing Mac OS comes from the NeXT OS and how much of the NeXT programming toolset makes up today's Mac/iPhone/iPad development toolset?  Is Apple a second incarnation of NeXT technology in some respects or not?  The book does not ponder these questions in enough detail.  If you believe, as I do, that the world has lost a brilliant thinker, then the book is worth reading to learn more about the man.  Hopefully there will be other accounts of Jobs' career as it seems that there is much more that could not be covered in the book's 600 pages.
B000PC0SPU|4.0|With A Whole New Mind, Mr. Pink is showing himself to be another Malcolm Gladwell: narrative non-fiction written in a very breezy, colloquial, yet effective style.  The book discusses the importance of right-brain thinking in today's society.  The prose is very straightforward and reads almost like an extended magazine article rather than a textbook or dry intellectual work.  Most people can probably knock this off in 3-4 sittings.  I won't go into an exhaustive discussion of content, as other reviewers have done that ad nauseum.  As a general description, Mr. Pink outlines specific right-brain qualities and why they are necessary for success. It features PREVIOUSLY UNRELEASED song material which Doris cut after a brief return to the recording studio during the mid-1980s.   These sessions took place after Doris Day's late son Terry Melcher convinced his dear Mom to return to the recording studio under his guise. Six of the new songs contained on MY HEART originate from these very sessions. Although a few of the recordings were aired during the eighties on the star's American chat show, \\"Doris Day's Best Friends\\", the majority were left in an \\"unfinished\\" state until they were polished and perfected by the multi-platinum selling British producer Ted Carfrae who recorded additional orchestrations to complete the songs.  Doris was fully involved in the music selections for MY HEART which is notably her first original album for over 17 years (the last being \\"[[ASIN:B000CS45PG Love Album]]\\"). As well as the eighties recordings there is also a studio recording taken from one of Day's 1970s US TV specials plus a trio of other Doris Day classics lifted from the vaults at Columbia - each of these were hand picked by the star for sentimental reasons. The album is dedicated to the memory of Ms. Day's son Terry Melcher.  In September 2011, the MY HEART album was released in the UK where it rocketed into the UK album chart at #9. As a result of its runaway success Doris Day became the oldest singer in UK chart history to have a TOP 10 hit with an album of new material. Although initially only available throughout Europe MY HEART is now available across the singer's home country of the USA thanks to her own label Arwin Productions. Better still for the \\"US edition\\" Doris added an exclusive bonus track STEWBALL - a duet with her son.  The MY HEART album packs a truly powerful punch on an emotional level as not only does it deliver some truly unforgettable vocal performances - such as YOU ARE SO BEAUTIFUL which will not leave a dry eye inthe house - but we are also all reminded from this album just how much we have missed Doris Day's voice and her music over the years she has been away!  This is a DAY-lightful comeback album (better late than never) and here's hoping if her American fans support it as much as her British ones did it will make a piece of pop history on the American BILLBOARD chart. 0393064476|3.0|This was an easy to read erudite book regarding the 1400's florentine scholar Poggio de Bracciolini, his dogged love for ancient latin literature that led him in search of classical texts abandoned in medieval  monasteries; the miracle that it was to find Lucretius poem \\"De Rerum Naturae\\" in an old German library and  the even greater miracle that it was to rescue it from the tides of time and  the turmoil of History. It was an uneasy  life that of the Renaissance with its  wars, plagues, the papacy and religious turmoil.    The influence of the poem in what is considered mainstream thought today-even though I am not sure it is as mainstream outside scientific and cultural circles as the author Mr. Greenblatt seems to assume- is what prompted the author to gush about  this little known chapter of scholarly history. He sees its influence in Thomas More (who tried to apply epicurean principles to a christian world view in his \\"Utopia\\") , Erasmus, Giordano Bruno, Shakespeare,  T. Jefferson and A. Einstein among others.  I think the author is right in assuming we live in a more epicurean society today but by far I think most of us are quite ensnared by other dogmas and thought shackles that might not be of religious nature per-se (Justin Bieber anyone? ) but are just as magical and superstitious.   The author also identifies the inner sorrow of  the scholars who pursued relief from the sordid world they endured  with the balm of their quest for ancient latin texts, a remnant of the grandeur of Rome, stripped even then of its stones and ambitions. But again, I think Mr. Greenblatt is depicting a bit of a flat picture with very little nuance beyond corrupt prelates, ignorant masses  and beleaguered scholars. I am sure, there were other avenues and works going on that would have made the reinassance the splendid period it was.   I also found interesting how the author mentions the many occasions in which the poem's incendiary message  was protected by the beauty of its language; to the point that most scribes and translators claimed to look upon it while dismissing  its content but fascinated by its beauty ( a claim meant to protect them from the Inquisition naturally).I would have liked to see more explanation on that point, more samples of the actual poem.     Epicurus was the founder of a doctrine of pleasure seekers in ancient Greece . Some Roman scholars had embraced its tenets as epicureans themselves. The Middle Ages saw the decline of some philosophical freedoms as ,over time, the Catholic Church had  taken upon itself to root out, mold and regulate  any idea that might threaten its dogma and its power. Paradoxically, monasteries were the last refuge of the  ancient classical texts sought by Poggio and  his competitors. Lucretius poem delivered the philosophical  ideal of Epicureanism and saw the light anew when such an ideal was tantamount to blasphemy. Sensible religious men perished at the stake  (jan Hus, Jerome of Prague) for crimes much milder than embracing epicurean doctrine.     The poem was leaps and bounds beyond , it  hinted at a time and a way of life that could ignore the Gods altogether or assume that, if they existed, they would care nothing for human affairs. The dedication of the poem to Venus just indicates that Lucretius saw the world as in constant erotic tension, a resplendent self-regulated chaos  where the ultimate goal is to find pleasure and avoid fear,  even pain. The poem paints a world where everything is in constant change, where the universe was not created , furthermore, it was not created for us . Its building blocks are \\"atomi\\" or minuscule particles. Its philosophy accepts there is no reward or punishment after death and that all we have is our time allotted to live. Far from being a pessimistic message that would induce everyone to just seek pleasure with no regards for others, it hints at a way of proper living in which the ultimate pleasures are obtained from the common happiness of all and the dismissal of magical thinking, superstition and useless grief. I am not going to attempt to explain Epicureanism in my review , suffice it to say , it has an appealing common sense to it.   In general, the book was well written and entertaining. In some areas it has a bit too much of the salacious anecdote and exaggerated claiming  typical of any book that wants to be in a best-seller list today but I din't mind that. It is part of the pleasure of reading.

0316042994|5.0|\\"Brideshead Revisited\\" is about a family and their religious beliefs, viewed from without, as every family and faith must be if they are to be viewed at all.  The narrator is, by turns, the friend of one sibling and the lover of another, but neither his affection nor his concern can grant him passage to the haunted chambers of the Brideshead mansion or guide him through the tortured relations its corridors resemble. It is a book about belief and religion, grace and wealth, eros and convention, culture and aristocracy--hence, the good and its shadow. Along with Greene's \\"The End of the Affair,\\" this is one of the English-speaking world's classic fictional meditations on the hold religious belief exercises on the human heart, regardless of the passions that pull it in other directions.  What sets Waugh apart (along with the likes of Joyce and McCarthy) from other great novelists is the sheer beauty of his prose.  Reading the first few pages never fails to move me to tears, and I don't know exactly why. But even the sound of the words in my mind is like the linguistic equivalent of a chocolate truffle, melting before you can savor it. Waugh writes like John Singer Sargent paints--with exquisite attention to every brushstroke--so that's it's not just the cumulative effect of the ideas which moves you, but the words and sentences themselves, as if the profundity of the story somehow overflows its bounds to leak back into every word with which it is told.

1451627289|5.0|I first read King when he and I were both still able to qualify as sweet, young things.  Young enough, in fact, that when I read one of his earliest novels - \\"Salem's Lot\\" - I was tucked up in a hospital bed after the birth of my oldest daughter, who is now grown and bringing up kids of her own.  I can honestly say it was the only book that ever scared me enough to keep me awake - and convince me that hospitals are spooky places at night.  Since that time, I've read most of his work, although not all.  And I've liked a lot of his stories, disliked a few of them, and fallen in love with a fair number.  I was nineteen years old on that fateful day in Dallas, and it lives with me still.  I can still recall the ice that formed in my heart when the radio announcement came on.  I can still remember what I was doing and who was there, and - above all - I can still remember the comment made by a woman who was a co-worker, but not a friend, and - after this occasion - never would be.  I won't repeat it here, but suffice to say I lived in the deep South, where JFK was NOT well-loved, and I have never again been as repulsed as when I heard what she said; my blood literally ran cold.  I was afraid this book might overlook that horrendous trauma, might make light of it and make all of us who were traumatized by the events of that day feel foolish.  It didn't.  After reading it (and taking it with the grain of salt that King himself recommends) I find that he's actually managed to do something I didn't think anyone would ever do.  He's settled my mind, at least enough to allow me to realize that the detailed truth may never be available to me (although rumor has it that the whole story will be told once everyone who might have been a part of those events is dead).  That pretty much leaves me out of it.  But I can finally conclude that Oswald probably WAS the lone assassin, even though others might have contributed to his madness.  As the old saying goes, sometimes a cigar is justa cigar, and Mr. King has provided sufficient documentation and imagination to allow me to accept that conclusion.  Of course, the fact that his writing is still like perfect music, demonstrating - above all - his adoration of the English language, makes it an easy, un-put-down-able read. It's amazing how quickly 800+ pages can go by when you're rapt and enchanted.  Thank you, Mr. King.  If this were the only manuscript residing on my Kindle, it would still be worth every dime.  Cyn

I am very serious, I debated over several weeks whether or not to just go ahead and read it, taking the risk that it might ruin the whole series for me (which the last 2 books were so beautifully written). Katniss gets knocked out way too much! Just when the scene is developing it's like BANG! She's either been shot, almost choked to death, literally on fire, or forcefully sedated. Even interferes in the deaths of those she loves  2. The deaths. Neither Katniss nor the readers are allowed a time for grievance unlike the previous books. Why is it that we feel so much more hurt over Rue's death than Prim's? Rue's death scene was moving and tear-jerking, even though we could see it coming. Prim's death was unexpected and it gets lost in the next page of Katniss' rambling about being on fire. Rue was someone who became Katniss' ally for a few days . Prim was Katniss' sister, the one she put up her own life for, the one she kept thinking about during the games, the one she's fought so long to keep alive. And then she's gone like that and I didn't feel anything. I am not saying Rue was not important, she was one of my favorite characters and probably one of the best scenes in the whole series, but just that Prim's death seems so off in this book compared to Rue's. And then Finnicks death and Boggs who I was just starting to really like, it's like poof gone! The end.  3. I hate to admit it but the whole Katniss love triangle gets played out by this 3rd book. I know your in the middle of crazy war confused and all but seriously, still trying to decide who your in love with? Really throughout the book Katniss is unfair to both Gale and Peeta, she really deserves to end up alone.  Ok the good of it all, the perfect pieces:  1. It's realistic if you really think about it. I think that was the problem for most people. The general population want happy stories and happy endings. As the reader we aren't really left with a \\"This is how it goes\\" ending. It's left up to us to decide. Is Katniss really happy? Has she lost her mind? Is she just living, but not really alive? What I was able to get out of it was that, Katniss nor Peeta nor anyone else who survived the wars will ever be the same. She's gone on with her life. I don't necessarily thinks she's happy with it but that she's at peace with it. She has someone who understands her, understands what she's been through and why she is the way she is. She has kids that won't have to compete in a cruel hunger games. Katniss ends up a lot better than she thought she would. She's making the best of her life even if it is unstable and broken, but she's making it.  2. Peeta. No matter how crazy and nutty he gets you can't help but love and feel sorry for him. But that's probably thanks to HG and Catching Fire. Even in an \\"hijacked\\" state of mind he's impossible to hate. Which makes you kind of turn on Katniss for getting so upset with him.   3. The underlying message of it all. No this story wasn't all about war but more than that. The book forces us to question ourselves in the same situations, or that's at least what it made me do.  Would I sacrifice my life for a loved one? For humanity?  What's really right? And who decides when it becomes wrong, when it's gone too far? Would you do something almost inhumane for the good of the world, if it meant defeating your enemies?(Dropping the bombs on the Capitol's children, the Hunger Games itself, making a final Hunger Game for the Capitol's children even though a huge reason for the uprising was to end the games, blowing up a mountain with your own allies in it just to get rid of your enemies, the torturing of the glam team for stealing bread)  What would you do through all this? Many of us would probably end up on morphling like Katniss, or fighters for the cause by any means necessary like Gale, losing who we are in the inside. Or maybe we might end up like Peeta (before he was hijacked) showing the Capitol that they haven't changed me or who I am. I hope I'd end up the latter. But who really knows until you find yourself in that situation.
