1451648537|5.0|So, Odd Jobs kicked the bucket! This Syrian Arab-American, with his Muslim-like cruelty in the way he treated his terrified employees, has been finally told to Shut Up!<br /><br />The book describes Jobs' early beginnings when his bio-father, a Syrian Arab, decided he was not worth keeping & put him up for adoption. Jobs never recovered from this act of being discarded. It affected his psyche for the rest of his life. He never forgave his Arab father either and refused to ever meet with him. Jobs was driven all his life to prove he was not worthless and he ran roughshod over anyone who got in the way of his achieving this goal.<br /><br />His first stroke of luck was to meet his friend Woz, who was technically a very competent hardware designer. Woz designed a motherboard which impressed Jobs. They decided to put the board into a box and call it the Apple II. They formed a partnership which lasted a lifetime. Jobs never cheated Woz the way Paul Allen said that Willy Gates cheated him. The Apple II was not much of a machine. The IBM PC was clearly superior.<br /><br />Jobs next started developing a desktop computer called the Mac. The Mac used the 68000 processor which was considered inferior to the Intel 8080 series. Jobs insisted in packaging the Mac in a curvy box with specially selected colors. He gave the machine an effeminate appearance. Since it did not use the Intel processor, it was never adopted by industry and did not sell well.<br /><br />Isaacson describes how Jobs was abandoned by his bio-father and how Jobs, in his turn, abandoned his own illegitimate daughter and did not show any love for her for the first 10 years of her life. Jobs' reptilian coldness towards this poor child, who never asked to be born, was grossly repulsive and unforgivable. His relationship to his two marital daughters was also lacking in warmth and love. The only person he wished to spend time with was his son Reed whom he loved very much.<br /><br />The development of the Mac was greatly delayed by the constant interference by Jobs in the hardware and software design process. Sales lagged and finally Jobs decided he needed to hire someone with organizational capability to straighten out the mess. He hired John Sculley who had an important position at Pepsico. Clearly this was a mistake since Pepsico has no connection to the electronics industry. After a couple of years Sculley convinced the Board of Directors to fire Jobs which they were happy to do.<br /><br />Some time passed during which Jobs connected with Disney to form a company called Pixar, which produced cartoon movies. During this time, Apple's performance deteriorated under Sculley's leadership and Jobs was asked to return to the company. From 1995 until this point, the Apple common stock had been in a coma. But in 2005, Jobs asked his engineers to improve on existing music players and to come out with the iPod. This was a big success and the stock started to rise. Next came the iPhone which was an improvement on existing cell phones. This device also sold well. These products were the result of the intelligence of the Apple engineers. Jobs contributed nothing except to have screaming sessions followed by extended sobbing about how the colors of the machine tools in the factory were not pretty and that there were not enough curves in the box containing the iPod and iPhone. The only reason that these very competent engineers tolerated this unpleasant verbal abuse and the autistic staring into the eyes of the individual being abused was - stock options. Many Apple employees became wealthy.<br /><br />The author describes frequently the neurotic beliefs of Jobs - his belief in Zen - the many trips to India to sit at the feet of some smelly snake-charmer and ask questions about how to live his life. Jobs absorbed all this nonsense and frequently fasted and ate weird vegan food products. One of the jokes told in the book describes the wish of guests invited to dinner at the Jobs homethat, when they leave, after dinner, they can find a restaurant open.<br /><br />The author describes the contempt that Jobs had for Willy Gates and Steve Balmer and Microsoft. He said that Gates stole all the ideas used in its product line. He said Gates never invented anything and that Microsoft would never be a great company again as long as Balmer was at the controls. He said that Gates is better suited to philanthropy than business.<br /><br />At some point, Jobs went for a MRI checkup and the doctor discovered a shadow on his pancreas. The doctor recommended immediate surgery to remove the cancer before it metastasized. Jobs, stuffed to the eyebrows with Zen nonsense, said \\"No, I do not want my body to be opened\\". Incredibly, he foolishly allowed nine months to go by before he had the surgery, despite his family and friends begging him to switch on his brain and do the right thing. By then the cancer had spread to his liver. He required a liver transplant, which he was able to get thanks to the private jet plane Apple gave him to play with.<br /><br />During the final years, Jobs worked hard improving products, starting the iStore & other activities that might take his mind off the pain he was suffering. He still continued to fast and eat strange foods. He still continued to interrupt his conversation and stare at people without speaking if they disagreed with him. He argued with his doctors and dismissed their opinions in a typically arrogant way. Eventually, things got to the point where he stayed in bed all day. He passed away surrounded by his family.n. B004AYCWY4|4.0|Bookmarks begins its review of this book by saying: \\"'Like a detective reconstructing a crime' (San Francisco Chronicle), Dawkins amasses a mountain of evidence in this richly illustrated, enormously readable explanation of the theory of evolution.\\" This book is Dawkins' effort to lay out the evidence for evolution. His target is the \\"intelligent design\\" approach to explaining why species end up as they are--explicitly rejecting Darwinian evolutionary theory.<br /><br />The book features many fascinating examples of evolution in action--including demonstrating that many species do NOT exhibit anything like intelligent design. His description of the wacky nerve structure of giraffes shows the willy nilly nature of evolution--and is certainly not consistent with the concept of intelligent design.<br /><br />Anyone familiar with other of Dawkins' works will not be surprised at what a nice read this book is. The author does write well and is persuasive throughout the book. However, those who believe in intelligent design are not apt to accept his arguments.<br /><br />For those who wish to get a sense of the logic of and evidence for evolution, this is a good starting point.

The basis of the book was to \\"be naked\\" in your presentations, which means to be real, honest, sincere, and to be free, direct, simple, passionate, as well as to be without barriers; but more importantly, it emphasized \\"connecting and engaging with an audience\\".   Chapter one of the book mentions a Japanese phrase \\"Hadaka no tsukiai, which means naked relationship and communication\\". The book elaborates on \\"Hadaka no tsukiai\\" in relation to the Japanese bath, which is a way of life and ritual to promote \\"healthy communication and good relationships\\".   The contributing speakers and/or authors all agreed with this, and suggested several ways to assist you be \\"naked\\". \\"The Naked Presenter\\" addresses preparation, removing barriers, engaging with the audience, being simple, leaving the audience with positive impressions and memorable information, as well as feeling good about your true self during and after the presentations.  Reynolds describes methods to become \\"naked\\" or better presenters. Some of these methods include proper tone pitch, rate of talk or speech, narrowing down your subject, utilizing story-telling, emotions, proximity to the audience, interacting with the audience, eye contact, and rehearsing instead of memorizing your presentation. In regards to Technology, the book reiterates less is best. The book suggests how the lighting should be, how big the font should be, and how many slides to use and how the slides should not only relate to your subject, but how the slides should not interfere with your audience's attention. The use of remote control devices is preferred in order to give the presenter more range of movement on stage or into the audience.  The book concludes with the importance of \\"The Three C's\\"; this is how Contribution to the audience, Connection with the audience, and Change of the audience's thinking, feelings, and actions should be the presenter's goal. After reading \\"The Naked Presenter\\", I am inspired to incorporate as much of the above methods, ideas, and suggestions to my future presentations and forms of communication.   I really enjoyed reading this book, and this book gave me a new insight and approach to presentations. I've been use to standing behind the podium and reading material from the screen or paper, and I will definitely incorporate what I've read and learned to be a better presenter.

King fans should love it, but this book could also bring in many more readers who weren't really a fan of King's horror novels. No horror here, just a little sci-fi, and a great story from a great storyteller.<br /><br />At over 800 pages, the plot is really quite simple. Jake Epping is living in 2011 when friend Al Templeton shows him something. Al has discovered a loop in time, a wormhole that allows him to travel back in time to 1958. Al has been going back it time for years and each time it takes him back to the exact time. Al came up with the idea of stopping John F. Kennedy's assassination, and convinced Jake that it was a worthwhile goal. So, the novel is about Jake going back in time to 1958, and living in the past for five years until the time comes to stop the assassination. Then, Jake plans to return to the present, where he will be five years older, but only two minutes will have passed in real time.<br /><br />Jake doesn't really begin trying to stop Lee Harvey Oswald until the middle of the novel. And that is what I enjoyed so much. You are taken on a journey where you meet many wonderful characters. Jake first tries to help a man from 2011 by preventing his dad from killing his siblings in 1958. Then he tries to save a young woman who is killed in a hunting accident. Then, he winds up south of Dallas in the town of Jodie, TX, where he becomes a school teacher, and meets the beautiful, but tall and clumsy Sadie.<br /><br />It is fascinating to witness Jake establish a life during the 1960s and all its romanticized glory. King takes away some of that luster by portraying the past as it really was, but still it is a fun journey to take. And then there is Lee Harvey Oswald. We get to know Lee and his family well just as Jake spends months witnessing Oswald's life, primarily to make sure he is acting alone.<br /><br />The last fifty pages are an exceptional ending to a great book. Loose ends are tied up, questions are explained, and I closed the back cover happy I read this novel. I must point out a few possible flaws in the novel. As a novel based on time travel, there really isn't that much. Jake spends the majority of time in one time period. This novel truly is more about stopping JFK's assassination than time travel and all its paradoxes. I must also point out that considering all the political ramifications surrounding Kennedy's assasination being prevented, King thankfully avoids preaching or offering up political commentary. This is just a fun story focused on an event that altered the course of history and forever changed the lives of those who lived throug it.at book. B0060I0IG2|4.0|It is rare these days for the major labels to invest in projects of this magnitude, a Beethoven cycle from the most important conductor active today and one of the oldest orchestras in the world. Everything about this release had an air of importance about it and it nice to see that there can still be excitement (whether genuine or manufactured) for yet another Beethoven cycle. But the question at the end of day is whether the cycle is a success. And the answer to that is, of course, yes and no.  There is no question that Chailly is a brilliant musician and that his band is a spectacular ensemble. There are too many fine ensemble moments to catalogue individually, but the sparkling allegretto from the Eighth, the ferocious trio from the Fifth's scherzo, or the entirety of the Pastorale bespeak just how magnificent this ensemble really is. The strings rich and plush, the winds idiomatic, and the brass, horns especially, offering biting commentary on these tried and true war horses. Tutti passages never sound routine as Chailly unearths a kalidscope of colors from his players, such as in the andante from the first or the magnificent Konig Stephan overture.  What is most thrilling about this set, however, is that Chailly has a lot to say about this music, nearly all of it good. Chailly's intelligence is evident in the amalgam of styles on display here. Clearly, any cycle from the Gewandhaus must pay homage to its place in the history of the German tradition, and in that sense, Chailly's big string section and dramatic flair are in keeping with the interpretations from Karajan, Wand, and Blomstedt. At the same time, Chailly has as much of the spirit of Toscanni in him, coupled with the latest scholarship from the historically informed and period groups, with his brisk tempos and hard-hitting forward focus. This is most evident in his adagio from the fourth, a full two minutes faster than Bohm's \\"traditional\\" approach with the Vienna Philharmonic, and the finales from that same symphony, along with those of the second and eighth. What we are left with is big band Beethoven that is taught, athletic, angular, idiomatic, and, oddly individualistic.  Chailly opts for the Peter's editions of the symphonies, rather than the recent Barenreiter editions, which might raise a few eyebrows, but it effects mostly how the music is delivered rather than the notes proper. The result is smart - trumpets playing the entire main theme in the Eroica's coda, horns playing throughout the finale bars of the Leonore overture, the \\"traditional\\" dynamic markings for the timpani in the scherzo of the ninth. Throughout, Chailly owns his own brilliant interpretive touches. These include some tinkerings with dynamics - subito fortepianos in cadence figures - unearthing oft-buried textures - the horns in the scene by the brook (!) - and imbuing the music with the extremes that make Beethoven so satisfying. This being Chailly, however, means that not all the choices work. Like Harnoncourt before him, Chailly is sometimes too much of an interventionist for his own good. The ritardando at the developmental climax of the first's allegro is purposeless while the diminuendo he slathers over the main theme of eighth's allegro run counter to Beethoven's contentions. Nor is Chailly string-heavy tonal palette always advantageous; strings smother wind details in the fifth's finale while the antiphonally placed second violins are sometimes obscured in tutti passages.  As far as the individual performances go, there are some real standouts, including a stellar Pastorale - brisk and brilliant, coupled with some of the most informative and idiomatic playing you are likely to hear. The First has the air of Toscanni and Wand about it, which is saying something, while the fourth and eighth sparkle with energy. The Eroica's funeral march is a gruesome affair while the entirety of the ninth is a success. The second, fifth, and seventh are all excellent performance, even if, when compared to Szell or Vanska, they lack that last ounce of cosmic energy.  Yet, despite this long list of success, the cycle falls a hair short of perfection due to Chailly's hard-hitting approach. This is less a function of speed than it is tension; Chailly does not quite \\"let go\\" the way he should in the cantabile slow movement of the fourth, the dolce trio of the seventh, or in second's larghetto. In some ways, despite its obvious tonal and spiritual linkages to Toscanni and Karajan, there is a lot of Solti on display here. Big moments are spectacularly impressive, but some of the more tender moments feel muscle bound. Of course, Chailly is a far more sensitive and idiomatic conductor than Solti - I show my prejudice here - but his fiercely driven and explosively forceful Beethoven may not suit all tastes, most especially those that have yet to warm to fast Beethoven.   With that sole reservation, this set is well worth exploring. Chailly offers so much and his earnest, honest musicianship is infectious. His body, mind, and soul are entirely invested in this music, which is exactly what Beethoven requires. Recommended!
B00260HH3K|1.0|the only thing they got right in this whole movie was the dragon balls but in my honest true opinon this is one of the worst movies ever made!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!

0321704452|5.0|\\"Standing up to recite information while others passively listen and perhaps take notes is the common and traditional presentation mode.  But it's an ineffective way to teach, inspire or motivate an audience.  If the lone goal is the transfer of information, you are better off distributing a handout and canceling the presentation.\\"  (Garr Reynolds, 2011)<br /><br />This paragraph was, in my opinion, one of the most powerful statements in the whole book.  It was also a reminder to me that the best presentations are the ones where the audience feels a connection to the presenter and they are receiving the information in a conversational style.  This is Garr Reynolds' opinion and I believe this as well.  The Naked Presenter was written by Garr to help individuals develop a presentation style that is pure, simple, direct, honest, and clear.  I really connected with this style and am excited to incorporate it into my future presentations.<br /><br />I found value in changing my delivery style from a performance mindset to more of a conversation format.  Presentations to me have always carried the weight of me, performing in front of a crowd. Garr's point is that we shouldn't focus on what we will present to the crowd but rather what the crowd needs to receive from the presentation.  When we do that we take the focus off of ourselves and put the focus on the audience.  That produces a much more beneficial outcome to the listener.<br /><br />We should focus on the beginning and the end of the presentation since that is what most audiences remember the most.  The opening should be so interesting that the audience is immediately interested in the topic.  Garr suggests using P.U.N.C.H (Personal, Unexpected, Novel, Challenging, Humorous) to do this.  I find that when speakers use a Personal approach or incorporate Humor into the presentation it is easier for a listener to relate and remain engaged in the subject.<br /><br />End with a powerful finish. This is something that I find valuable in preparing speeches but can at times forget this crucial part.  I thought that Garr's point about reinforcing the central theme after a Q&A session was spot on.  If you don't do that then the audience has a chance to walk out and miss the main point of the presentation.  This can be remedied by summarizing the main points, using a story or memorable quote to inspire the audience to further action.<br /><br />Overall a great read and I highly recommend this book for anyone who needs a fresh approach to presentations.s but can at times forget this crucial part. If you don't do that then the audience has a chance to walk out and miss the main point of the presentation.  This can be remedied by summarizing the main points, using a story or memorable quote to inspire the audience to further action.   Overall a great read and I highly recommend this book for anyone who needs a fresh approach to presentations.

B0007KTBIU|3.0|Wow and wow. Disney certainly doesn't restrain itself on this one. This movie plays out more like a Disney parody than an actual film. John Smith is a tall Prince Charming type with flowing blond hair, a commanding voice and the bravery of ten men but nothing beats the introduction of Pocahontas. The camera pans up to her standing in a dramatic pose on a rocky outcrop overlooking the land while the wind whips colorful leaves around her. Since she's Native American she can literally talk to the animals including her raccoon friend, Meeko. A girl in a canoe calls to her and I'm thinking, \\"she's definitely going to dive the football field length down into the lake\\". To my surprise she turns to run down the mountain... before doubling back and doing a flawless dive into the lake. OF COURSE John Smith and Pocahontas will end up together; they are both perfect. It's not enough for Pocahontas to commune with the animals, she literally talks to the trees and they talk back... with faces. Oh, is that not enough for you? She also gets messages from the wind. Still not convinced Pocahontas is perfection? She is able to learn English instantly, no explanation given. She even understands terms she couldn't possibly have any frame of reference for understanding. Rocks and bushes glow at her touch, raccoons braid her hair and she's able to ride on the wind.<br /><br />Pocahontas is hundreds of years of white guilt drawn up into one flawless Native American princess. It's is an 81 minute exaltation to the Disney Pocahontas; the Reductio Ad Absurdum of \\"Disney Magic\\". The actual Pocahontas died in her early 20's married to a tobacco planter named John Rolfe. There is a wisp of a story, a villain and some obnoxious animal sidekicks but they exist merely to complete the Disney formula and pad out the story. This is a story about how freaking AWESOME Native American's are and how only John Smith was worthy of being in their presence. Smith even manages to see the talking tree. There is a song near the end of the movie where the Native American's are shown to be as bigoted as the whites but I doubt the audience is fooled. The Native American's clearly have the moral superiority in this film.<br /><br />So I hated the movie, correct? Actually no. Pocahontas is such a parody in and of itself that you can just bask in its unapologetic unrestrained self indulgence. Like Superman or Mickey Mouse, Pocahontas the character has no depth and no personal conflict but there can be a certain joy and simplicity in such unimpeachable goodness. She isn't a human she's an archetype of female strength and kindness. When her friend Nakoma confesses that she sent Kocoum to save her leading to his death Pocahontas never ever for a fraction of a second blames Nakoma. She is simply beyond human flaws. If you want to be awash in everything that people love and hate about Disney this is the movie to see.<br /><br />Financially Pocahontas was a huge success and it has two really good songs but critically it was pretty middle of the road. I really didn't have a problem with any of the voice acting and somehow through the whole film I never picked up on the fact that Smith was voiced by Mel Gibson. Smith's friend Thomas was voiced by a young Christian Bale. To Disney's credit they showed a shocking burst of historical accuracy by having Pocahontas NOT end up with John Smith and in the sequel she correctly ends up with John Rolfe. Pocahontas is sort of a turning point where Disney reached its extreme before pulling back. After this movie Disney started slowly but surely changing the formula culminating in some wonderful films like The Emperor's New Groove and Lilo & Stich. These films were more highly acclaimed but less successful financially. So what is the lesson here? 0061251321|5.0|To begin the authors define a tribe as \\"a group between 20 and 150 people. Here's the test for whether someone is in one of your tribes: if you saw her walking down the street, you'd stop and say \\"hello\\"\\". The continue: \\"Tribes in company get work done - sometimes a lot of work - but they don't form because of work. Tribes are the basic building block of any large human effort, including earning a living. As such their influence is greater than that of teams, entire companies, and even superstar CEOs. In companies, tribes decide whether the new leader is going to flourish or get taken out. They determine how much work gets done, and of what quality.\\" The key question is then what makes the difference between tribes that excel and others that do not? The authors argue it is the presence of Tribal Leaders. The continue by defining what tribal leaders do: \\"Tribal Leaders focus their efforts on building the tribe - or more precisely, upgrading the tribal culture...Divisions and companies run by Tribal Leaders set the standard of performance in their industries, from productivity and profitability to employee retention. They are talent magnets, with people so eager to work for the leader that they will take a pay cut if necessary...Their efforts seem effortless, leaving may people puzzled by how they do it.  Many Tribal Leaders, if asked can't articulate what they are doing that's different, but after reading this book, you will be able to explain and duplicate their success.\\"<br /><br />The book's main focus after having defined the tribe and Tribal Leadership is to \\"give you perspective and tools of a Tribal Leader: someone who can unstick the conveyor belt - and make it run faster  for whole groups of people, no matter which stage they're in. The result is more effective workplaces, greater strategic success, less stress, and more fun.\\" This conveyor belt is an analogy for what the authors define as the tribal stages 1 through 5. Each stage is characterized by certain language and behavior.<br /><br />Stage 1: \\"The person at Stage One is alienated from others, expressing the view that \\"life sucks.\\"<br /><br />Stage 2: \\"Stage Two people are surrounded by people who seem to have some power they lack. As a results, their language expresses \\"my life sucks.\\"<br /><br />Stage 3: \\"The person at Stage Three is connected to others in a series of dyadic (two-person) relationships. the language of this stage expresses \\"I'm great,\\" and in the background - unstated - is \\"and you're not.\\"<br /><br />Stage 4: \\"The person forms structures called triads, in which they build values-based relationships between others. At the same time, the words of Stage Four people are centered on \\"we're great\\" and, in the background, \\"and they're not.\\" The \\"they\\" is another tribe - in the same company or in another.<br /><br />Stage 5: \\"A person at Stage Five expresses \\"life is great.\\" Five shares the same characteristics of Four, except that there is no \\"they.\\" As a result, these people form ever-growing networks with anyone whose values resonate with their own. The only Stage Five cultures we have observed (in corporate settings) exists as long as a history-making project lasts or as long as the tribe is so far ahead of its competitors that they are irrelevant.\\"<br /><br />Simply put the role of Tribal Leaders is \\"do two things: (1) listen for which cultures exist in their tribes and (2) upgrade those tribes using specific leverage points.\\"<br /><br />A very interesting, educative and fun read. It helps one look at companies through a new viewpoint with a specific focus on the culture and relationships/dynamics within it. It is filled with practical real-life examples and applications and backed by substantial empirical research. Highly recommended!<br /><br />Below are excerpts from the book that I found particularly insightful:<br /><br />1- \\"People at Stage Three approach leadership as though it were a set of tasks they could check off their to-do list (e.g., \\"set the vision,\\" \\"get alignment,\\" and \\"listen with intention\\"). The moment leadership becomes cookie-cutter, it isn't leadership at all - it's management. By making the person aware that he's behaving in a Stage Three fashion toward leadership, you might help him see that he isn't a leader at all. This realization may propel him into the set of epiphanies of the next chapter.\\"<br /><br />2- \\"...The two most important aspects of owning Stage Four: identifying and leveraging core values, and aligning on a noble cause. Everything else the tribe does should be sandwiched between these constructs. Projects, activities, initiatives, processes - unless they are fueled by values and reach toward the tribal vision - should either be rethought until they are consistent with these guiding principles, or pruned. By definition, core values and a noble cause can never be \\"checked off,\\" in the same way that companies complete an upgrade to computer technology.\\"<br /><br />3- \\"...Values must be core, and that means universal...Second, the unity resulting from core cause and a noble cause must be alignment, not agreement...Alignment, to us, means bringing pieces into the same line - the same direction.\\"<br /><br />4- \\"The Tribal Leadership Strategy Map: Start with core values and noble cause in the center, then move to outcomes and go counterclockwise around the model (assets and behaviors). Test Questions: Assets sufficient for the Outcomes? Enough assets for behaviors? Will behaviors accomplish outcomes?\\"<br /><br />5- \\"An outcome, by contrast (to a goal), is  a present state of success that morphs into an even bigger victory over time.\\"<br /><br />6- \\"A stage five tribe can work with any group that has a commitment to values that are core and that apply to everyone, even if those values are different from its own.\\"<br /><br />7- \\"While Tribal Leaders do their work for the good of the group, not for themselves, they are rewarded with loyalty, hard work, innovation, and collaboration. The tribe gets work of higher quality done in less time. The person is often seen as a candidate for op organizational jobs or for positions in government.\\"okie-cutter, it isn't leadership at all - it's management. By making the person aware that he's behaving in a Stage Three fashion toward leadership, you might help him see that he isn't a leader at all. This realization may propel him into the set of epiphanies of the next chapter.\\"  2- \\"...The two most important aspects of owning Stage Four: identifying and leveraging core values, and aligning on a noble cause. Imagine my delight when I walked into my local library and a copy was sitting on the shelf. I scooped it up and hurried home to devour it.<br /><br />Unfortunately, A Discovery of Witches ended up being one of those books that did not live up to the hype. Not even in the least. I will give Deborah Harkness credit for the only thing I can say I enjoyed about this novel: the atmosphere. Harkness created a highly atmospheric novel that was very rich in detail and setting. I truly enjoyed reading about the different places: the old libraries, the ancient manors, etc. Harkness crafted this portion of the novel very well.<br /><br />What I did not like was pretty much everything else. I found the characters to be just terrible. Extremes in the characters are really what ruined them for me. Diana Bishop goes from simpering academic (which I did not like) to over-the-top, all sources of magic in the whole wide world uber-witch. The novel starts off with Diana refusing to acknowledge her witchcraft heritage and powers. Then, her acceptance is thrown into hyper-drive and not only is she an accepting witch, she had every super power known to mankind and a few that have never before been seen. It was way too much. I am highly accepting of magical worlds. Magical worlds make up the bulk of what I read. But when a character goes from a simple, magical creature, to being so over-the-top powerful that is borders on ridiculous, well, you've lost me. This is a prime example of why I stopped reading Laurell K. Hamilton's Meredith Gentry series. I do not enjoy conflated characters.<br /><br />Matthew Clairmont was just meh. He's a vampire, he falls in love with Diana, blah blah blah. I didn't find him engaging or interesting. He was overly macho, which I find repulsive in characters. I get that he is an ancient vampire and maybe he has some older sensibilities, but again, those sensibilities were overblown and made me dislike him.<br /><br />The plot was interesting at first. However, once the plot shifted focus from the magical document to the romance between Diana and Matthew, I couldn't have cared less. Add into that my extreme displeasure with the uber-nature of everything about Diana and Matthew, and it's not shocking why I disliked this novel. Also, this book could have been about 200 pages shorter and still told the same story. Characters were not the only things inflated by Harkness.<br /><br />I wouldn't recommend this book to anyone. I absolutely will not be reading any of the other books in this series. I am very glad I got this book from the library and didn't spend a dime on it. 0374275637|4.0|By many accounts, Daniel Kahneman is the father of his field, Behavioral Economics, which is a mix between behavioral psychology and traditional economics and has provided perhaps the most fascinating material in recent pop science literature. While this book is no exception in that regard, it is plagued by the same deficiencies that other Behavioral Economics books are plagued by, and so doesn't quite accomplish the potential of the new science.  The premise behind Behavioral Economics is that people are irrational. Countering the standard of the `rational agent model', in which it is assumed that people think and act rationally based on their own interests, behavioral economists have shown that people consistently make decisions that are uneconomical and often self-defeating. The conclusion is that people cannot possibly be rational and that something else is going on.  Daniel Kahneman's thesis is that everyone possesses two systems of thought--the first is automatic, emotional, and intuitive (`fast' as he summarizes it); the second is deliberate, logical, and controlled (`slow'). This dichotomy means that we have the capacity for both reasoning and emotional functions; it also means that we are prone to mistakes. Kahneman shows how the two systems of thought are engaged and what happens when the two mix. Kahneman argues that we define ourselves by our `System 2' slow thinking, but in reality, we are largely governed by our `System 1' fast thinking. This is where a number of cognitive biases and errors come in.  Kahneman's work on these biases is legendary. As a part of a pioneering duo with Amos Tversky, he laid out a number of experiments that show how people consistently make faulty errors in all kinds of situations--at the grocery store, in the classroom, at the voting booth, etc. It is from these studies and other similar work that pop psychology issues such as `anchoring', `framing', `loss aversion', `hindsight bias', and others were introduced. Now--thanksin large part to Kahneman and this book--they are part of our everyday conversations.  Kahneman covers all of these concepts in this book, and does so par excellence since he has had such an integral part in forming them. And, the material is as fascinating as it is in any other Behavioral Economics book. But, as it is with other Behavioral Economics books, the argument is contingent on a number of premises that cannot be assumed, and so conclusions reached end up being far-fetched and rather illogical in themselves.  To begin, the theory has holes. It is argued that, since System 2 is where we conduct our rational choices, System 1 must therefore be non-rational or irrational. And, since we are largely governed by System 1, our actions are based on non-rational processes. But Kahneman does not explain how the different systems arise, nor does he examine what the systems are made up of; he simply shows that people think and make decisions in two different manners. Here, Kahneman shows that System 1 is quick and based on limited information, but this doesn't mean that it is irrational--impetuous and uninformed, maybe, but it still can be based in logic.  One would be right to question the method as well. Kahneman's research is largely based in thought experiments, psychological experiments, and surveys. While this doesn't mean that his conclusions are automatically false, it does mean that his findings are limited--a fact that he fails to guard against.  For example, throughout the book Kahneman would examine an experiment, determining that a group of people acted a certain way under certain circumstances, and then claim that the findings apply to everyone including the reader. An experiment described early in the book showed how a group of people were less helpful on average after being `primed' by the concept of money. Kahneman then says \\"You have no choice but to accept that the major conclusions of these studies are true. More important, you must accept that theyare true about you. If you had been exposed to a screen saver of floating dollar bills, you too would likely have picked up fewer pencils to help a clumsy stranger.\\" But this is a simple whole-to-part fallacy. Kahneman argues that because a group of people was influenced by a certain prime that all people would be influenced by that same prime. The claim cannot be logically defended--there are exceptions to all of these rules, and so the conclusion cannot be so absolute.  All of the studies and conclusions in this book are subject to similar criticism. This doesn't make the studies uninteresting or banal in any way. On the contrary, the material is that much more interesting when the reader realizes that there is something missing in the analysis. The intellectual agitation encourages a rebuttal in long form, which is on its way. In the meantime, enjoy this survey of some of the most fascinating material on human behavior.

B004ZNB12O|2.0|As an earlier reviewer said, the star rating is directed SOLELY at this album, not at any of the music herein or at Pink Floyd in general.  The music itself is absolutely five stars, and only because I can't give it more!  A decade ago in 2001, Pink Floyd released Echoes, a two-disc best-of collection.  This set had just enough of the old Barrett-era Floyd, most of the classics from the modern-day albums, and one semi-rarity (When The Tigers Broke Free, only later added to The Final Cut album in 2004).  While by no means perfect, it at least represented much of the Floyd's history, and included both of the vital early singles, See Emily Play and Arnold Layne.  These two tracks are also on two earlier best-of collections, Relics (1971) and Works (1983).  Works also has one track not available on any other Floyd album, titled Embryo, as well as a different mix of Brain Damage/Eclipse.  A Foot In The Door covers the same ground as Echoes, but far less impressively.  A single disc is never going to surpass a double, but by including nearly all of the same tracks, this set is essentially redundant.  The only tracks on A Foot In The Door but not Echoes are Have A Cigar and Brain Damage/Eclipse.  Being as Echoes is still readily available on Amazon.com and other online outlets, how does one justify purchasing A Foot In The Door?  The public would have been far better served if the Floyd had followed Led Zeppelin's example from a few years back, when Zeppelin released two single-disc compilations titled Early Days and Latter Days.  The Early Days compilation could have been basically Relics and Works intermingled plus a few more album cuts, with Dark Side of the Moon being the cutoff.    The Latter Days compilation could have started with Wish You Were Here.  It could have included, perhaps, a couple of live cuts from Delicate Sounds Of Thunder, Pulse, Is There Anybody Out There?, and maybe even one of the songs from Live 8.    A deluxe set with both discs could have had athird disc of rarities such as Embryo, Apples And Oranges, Come In Number 51 Your Time Is Up and Heart Beat Pig Meat from the Zabriskie Point soundtrack, Raving And Drooling, the alternate version of Empty Spaces from The Wall movie soundtrack, possibly a cut or two from Live At Pompeii, and maybe some more samples from the Household Objects project that never happened.  This would have been the version I'd have bought.   Perhaps the band is out there reading these reviews; if so, guys, I think you'd find my suggestion to be a big seller in your catalog.  In the meantime, I hate to be so harsh on a Pink Floyd release, but this album, in my opinion, is barely a step up from the disaster that is 1981's A Collection Of Great Dance Songs.  Seek out Works, Relics, and Echoes for the good Pink Floyd best-of collections.

B005K0HDGE|5.0|I almost feel like Stephen King and I are old friends, and have been since I first read Salem's Lot many years ago.  In fact, I think it would be safe to say that I have, with the exceptions of the last Dark Tower books and The Shining - the latter of which so completely freaked me out I have never been able to finish either the novel or either of the two theatrical presentations - rad pretty much everything the Crown Prince of Bangor, ME has written.<br /><br />Not long ago in this column I reviewed the deliciously fun Kindle single, UR.  Even when King doesn't bundle his short stories, they're a blast.<br /><br />Stephen King has demonstrated over the years that you can't pigeonhole him into any genre.  While it is certainly true that horror is his stock in trade, publications like UR, The Girl who Loved Tom Gordon, The Eye of the Dragon, Danse Macabre, and On Writing, just to name a few, clearly demonstrate that when Stephen King steps outside the box he does so with aplomb!  11/22/63 is just such an `outside the box' novel.<br />Jake Epping is a high school English teacher in Lisbon Falls, Maine who earns a little extra pocket change teaching adult night classes for those long past their high school years who want to earn their G.E.D.  Once such student is the Lisbon high janitor, Harry Dunning, whose poorly written essay on life changing events touches Jake in ways he never expected.  You might even say that Harry Dunning's watershed moment became Jake Epping's watershed moment.<br /><br />On the night Harry receives his G.E.D. diploma, Jake treats him to dinner at an eatery rarely frequented by Lisbon Falls students and apart from Jake himself, never by the faculty; a little place called Al's Diner, home of the Fatburger.  Over time, Al and Jake have become friends and when the time is both pressing and right, Al invites Jake to experience a hidden mystery in the diner's pantry.  \\"Close your eyes and keep walking - it makes it easier\\" is one of several instructions Al gives Jake.  When he finally opens his eyes, Jake finds himself standing on a spit of land that will one day be Al's Diner's pantry, but whatever piece of 2011 it had been when Jake entered Al's pantry, it is now 11:58 am the morning of September 9, 1958.<br /><br />When Jake returns back to the pantry through what Al calls the `rabbit hole' as in Alice in Wonderland, Al ultimately tells Jake what he wants of him.  Go back through the rabbit hole, stay there for a little over five years, and stop Lee Harvey Oswald from assassinating John Fitzgerald Kennedy in Dallas on November 22nd, 1963 - 11/22/62.<br /><br />If I tell you anything else I'll give away too much so I'm going to stop with the story synopsis.  In its place I'll tell you why you want to buy this book.  11/22/63 is perhaps the best piece of writing ever by a master story teller at the top of his craft.  It is none of what I expected from the pen of Stephen King, and yet it is all I could want and more.  Dabbling in science fiction questions about quantum mechanics, time travel paradoxes, and hints of the usual King fare, what you get in the end is a superbly crafted tale that twists in and out of the Oswald mystique, dances around the conspiracy theories, and tosses in a few surprises I never saw coming.  This is vintage King and more.  His first person narrative from the point of view of Jake Epping leaves you with the sense of sitting around the great hall hanging expectantly on every word while The king's master bard wields the sword and staff of his well-chosen words in a story both captivating and emotive.<br /><br />I don't think it would be too much of a stretch to suggest that 11/22/63 may very well be the best work of fiction Stephen King has ever written, and certainly the best thing I have read this year.  Can I give 6 stars out of 5?  It's really that good!<br /><br />Believe me when I say this is the best book of 2011, and quite possibly King's best effort since he began writing.You will not want to put it down, and this is a book you will most definitely add to your `keeper' collection.ely add to your `keeper' collection.

B005HJWWW8|5.0|The Fiio E6 Headphone Amplifier is a great device for those who want louder, better music. The thing that bothered me before I bought this product, however, is the lack of explanation of what the 3 setting equalizer does. There is a small round light at the back of the amplifier showing either red, blue, both, or none (off). The color relates to which equalizer setting is being used: Red is higher bass, blue is even higher volume output, both colors seems to only lessen the volume. When the colors are off, only the amplifier's volume setting affects the music output (Changed with the + and - buttons on the side). With the amplifier's volume on maximum, and the equalizer on blue (higher volume), the total volume output seemed to have been doubled compared to not using the amplifier. Overall, I love this device, I only wish the information I've provided about the equalizer was available before I had purchased it.
